Title: Jasmine Bud Worm
Authors: Manju   Thakur
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    The females
lay eggs on the unopened bud, calyx and bud stalk.&nbsp;The newly emerged
larva is creamy yellow in colour whereas the fully grown larva is greenish
with pale body hairs and a black head. The larva bores into immature jasmine
buds and feeds on floral structures. The adult is a small white moth with
black wavy lines on its hind wings and abdomen. Infested flowers turn pale
red in colour and then fall off the plant.
    The
jasmine bud worm is a caterpillar which, during its initial stages, makes
holes on the flower bud and feeds on its inner floral structures. The larva
usually attacks 2 to 3 buds. The larvae come out through a small hole made in
the buds to attack another bud in the same shoot. In severe infestation, the
larva makes a web-like pattern among the adjacent buds and feeds on petals.
    <ul>
    <li>
    Collect and destroy larvae and infested
    flowers at least once a week</li>
    <li>Proper pruning and hygienic
    maintenance of the bushes helps to minimize the pest incidence</li>
    <li>Set up light traps to attract and
    kill the adult moths</li>
    <li>Spray NSKE 5% or malathion 50EC
    2ml/litres</li>
    <li>Spray one of the following
    insecticides for controlling the pest viz., profenofos 50 EC at 1 ml or
    thiacloprid 240 SC at 1 ml per litre of water to control the pest population and
    to minimize the economic loss</li>
</ul>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Hendecasis duplifascialis</em>
Images: 581e2230-73c5-4f21-8ba0-e80c161d745e.jpg
Content:
The females
lay eggs on the unopened bud, calyx and bud stalk.&nbsp;The newly emerged
larva is creamy yellow in colour whereas the fully grown larva is greenish
with pale body hairs and a black head. The larva bores into immature jasmine
buds and feeds on floral structures. The adult is a small white moth with
black wavy lines on its hind wings and abdomen. Infested flowers turn pale
red in colour and then fall off the plant. The
jasmine bud worm is a caterpillar which, during its initial stages, makes
holes on the flower bud and feeds on its inner floral structures. The larva
usually attacks 2 to 3 buds. The larvae come out through a small hole made in
the buds to attack another bud in the same shoot. In severe infestation, the
larva makes a web-like pattern among the adjacent buds and feeds on petals. <ul>
    <li>
    Collect and destroy larvae and infested
    flowers at least once a week</li>
    <li>Proper pruning and hygienic
    maintenance of the bushes helps to minimize the pest incidence</li>
    <li>Set up light traps to attract and
    kill the adult moths</li>
    <li>Spray NSKE 5% or malathion 50EC
    2ml/litres</li>
    <li>Spray one of the following
    insecticides for controlling the pest viz., profenofos 50 EC at 1 ml or
    thiacloprid 240 SC at 1 ml per litre of water to control the pest population and
    to minimize the economic loss</li>
</ul> When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.

Title: Management of Parawilt Disease in Cotton
Authors: Vijay S Thokre
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    The incidence is particularly
high in plants with a large canopy and heavy boll load. Leaves of affected
plants show wilt like drooping, become chlorotic and turn bronze or red
followed by drying. Premature dropping of leaves and fruiting parts may occur.
Leaves lose turgidity and become wilted due to increased water loss. Squares
and young bolls are shed and immature bolls are forcefully opened. Wilted
plants show development of anthocyanin pigment, giving a red appearance. Most
of the wilted plants gradually recover and produce new flushes but their
contribution to yield is small.
    Parawilt causes considerable
concern amongst cotton growers across the country. It is also known as sudden
wilt. Unlike pathogenic wilt, which occurs in groups of plants in fields, it is
difficult to quantify the incidence and the yield loss of parawilt. Detailed
investigations on isolation, distribution pattern and pathogen transmission
proved that fungi, bacteria, viruses and nematodes were not involved in this
problem. The cause of Parawilt could not be proven until recently, mainly
because of its random occurrence and inability to simulate the wilt under
artificial conditions.<br>
</br>
These plants exhibit high
photosynthesis and transpiration (water loss) rates. Consequently, for the production
of proteins and other food, these plants require a high uptake of nutrients.
Nutrient uptake, which is an active process and inhibited under anaerobic
(water-logged) conditions. As a result of the feedback inhibition of root
respiration, the root system degenerates in wilted plants. Damage to the roots
affects their ability to take up and move water to the rest of the plant. This,
coupled with higher transpiration in wilted plants due to their insensitive
stomata, causes parawilt in cotton.
    <ul>
    <li>
    Provide adequate drainage to avoid water logging of the fields</li>
    <li>Irrigation may be provided during grand growth phase to avoid prolonged
    exposure of plants to dry conditions</li>
    <li>Apply 2% urea through foliar spray</li>
    <li>Apply copper oxychloride
    25g per 10 litres water by drenching near the root zone of infested and healthy
    plants</li>
    <li>Apply cobalt chloride 1g/ 100 litres water immediately after the appearance
    of symptoms
    </li>
</ul>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.

Title: Pod Fly in Pigeon pea
Authors: T.   Aruna
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    The adult is small black fly and
is about 5 mm in length. Eggs are laid in the wall of an immature pod. The
female pod fly lays individual eggs on the developing pods. The pod fly
infested pods do not show any external symptom of damage until the fully-grown
larvae chew the pod wall. They leave behind a thin, papery membrane called a
window, through which adults exit the pods. The white maggots feed on the
developing grain and pupate inside the pod. The pupae bore the grains and make
the tunnel in them. The damaged grains do not germinate and are unfit for human
consumption.
    The pod fly is a major pest in pigeon pea. Infestation
of pod flies causes damage to 20-80% of the crop. The pod fly attack remains
unnoticed by the farmers owing to the hidden lifestyle of the insect and thus
it becomes difficult to manage.
    <ul>
    <li>
    Sow the crop early in the season</li>
    <li>Use resistant varieties</li>
    <li>Intercrop with Jowar (sorghum), maize and groundnut</li>
    <li>Crop rotation should be practiced</li>
    <li>Conservation of natural enemies</li>
    <li>Spray
    Quinalphos 25 EC @ 1.6 ml/lit or Phenthoate
    50 EC @ 1.4 ml/lit of water at 50 % flowering stage and after 10 - 15 days neem
    seed kernel extract should be used
    </li>
</ul>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Melanagromyza obtusa</em>
Images: e0daef2f-f725-4ad6-b255-71beb6f51c9e.jpg, b4fcfbd0-daef-4182-8d5c-5889463b4b44.jpg
Content:

Title: Powdery mildew in Green gram
Authors: Vaishali   Londase
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    A powder appears in patches on
leaves and other green parts of the plant. This powder is white but later turns
a dull colour. Gradually, the powdery patches grow and become circular until
they also cover the lower surface of the leaf. In severe infections, leaves
turn yellow and fall early. Severely affected parts shrivel and become
distorted. The disease causes forced maturity and therefore reduced crop yields.
    This is a major yield-limiting factor in green
gram. Among the various diseases, powdery mildew is a very common problem in
cool, dry weather. This pathogen is an obligate parasite and has a wide host
range. It survives in conidial form on
various hosts in the off-season. The losses caused by powdery mildews in India are
tremendous, particularly in the yield of peas, grapes, cereals and cucurbits.
    <ul>
    <li>
    Diseased
    debris should be collected and burnt in the field</li>
    <li>Early
    varieties should be preferred</li>
    <li>Sowing
    must be done early in the month of June to avoid early incidence of the disease</li>
    <li>Powdery
    mildew could be controlled by spraying Carbendazim (0.05%) and Penconazole
    (0.05%)</li>
    <li>Two
    sprays of Tridemorph 1 ml per litre; one dose immediately after the disease
    appearance and the second dose 15 days later</li>
</ul>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Erysiphe betae</em>
Images: 6c26cc72-a02d-441c-8db3-83589078442d.jpg, cf6d46d7-ca23-4b37-a13c-0f44e6179491.jpg

Title: Cercospora Leaf Spot in Sesame
Authors: Vaishali   Londase
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    Sesame is an important
oilseed crop. The crop suffers from many fungal, bacterial, viral and
phytoplasmal diseases. Cercospora
leaf spot, caused by the fungus&nbsp;<em>Cercospora
sesami,</em>&nbsp;infects all above
ground parts of the plant, resulting in complete defoliation which leads to
severe economic losses. The disease, which affects leaves as early as 4 weeks
after planting, starts as small pinhead-sized cottony spots on the infected
leaves. These spots gradually spread on the lamina and can extend up to 4 mm in
diameter. Extensive infection leads to defoliation and damage of capsules before
the plant reaches maturity which can result in yield losses of 20 to 50%.
    The fungal spores are
spread to healthy plants through rain, irrigation water and wind. Germination
occurs in humid conditions, usually during late spring and summer, and fungus growth
is encouraged when leaves are frequently damp.
    <ul>
    <li>
    Carry
    out field sanitation and destroy crop residues</li>
    <li>Treat
    seeds with thiram or carbendazim @ 2 g per Kg of&nbsp; seed</li>
    <li>Early
    planting should be done i.e. planting immediately after the onset of the
    monsoon</li>
    <li>Follow
    intercropping system of sesame + pearl millet (3:1)</li>
    <li>Use
    a resistant variety, such as RT-127
    &sect;&nbsp; Apply
    a foliar spray (2-3 times) of wettable sulphur 80 % wp (0.2%) at 10 day
    intervals from when the disease appears</li>
    <li>Apply three
    sprays of Mancozeb (0.25 %) at 15 day intervals from when disease appears</li>
</ul>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Cercospora sesami</em>
Images: 3f2e24f5-1121-4798-9c58-8767feda1721.jpg, a977a98f-3b10-42b3-86e4-0dfca5baef76.jpg
Content:

Title:

Title: Red Palm Weevil of Coconut
Authors: Mr   Murugan
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    The red

palm weevil is one of the major pests infesting palm crops like coconut, sago and

other palm trees. On the infested trees, a reddish

brown liquid is seen oozing out from the hole in the stem close to the ground. The liquid gives off a rotten smell.
    The red palm weevil is one of the

most destructive pests of coconut. The grubs cause damage inside the stem and

crown by feeding on soft tissues. When the infestation is severe, the inside

portion of trunk is completely eaten and replaced by rotten fibres.
    <ul>

    <li>

    Dig

    the soil deeply around the coconut trunk in April-May to make a trench for the grubs

    to fall in to. Hand-pick the grubs and kill them.</li>

    <li>Cut

    down heavily infested trees and destroy the various stages of the pest</li>

    <li>Use

    pheromone traps @ 12 / ha to effectively bring down the pest population</li>

    <li>Mix

    mashed rotten banana with 1.5 litres buttermilk and stir well. Pour this liquid

    into a plastic bucket and cover tightly, leaving a small hole of 3 X 3 cm in

    the lid&nbsp;</li>

    <li>Fill

    the wounds on the trees and leaf axis with a mixture of 10% carbaryl and clay

    (1:1). The rest of the holes on the trees should be closed prior to the

    treatment so that the pest does not escape from them.</li>

    <li>Select

    young feeder roots by digging around the tree trunk. Mix a root feeding of carbosulfan

    25% EC @ 10 ml/ 15 ml of water.&nbsp; Cut the

    root at an angle to give it a slanting cut. The cut portion of the root is then

    immersed in the insecticidal solution kept in polythene bags so that the cut

    end is dipped up to the bottom of the polythene bag.&nbsp; The polythene bag is then tied with jute

    string. The root absorbs the entire quantity within four days.</li>

    <li>Select

    young, vigorously growing roots for applying insecticide after harvesting of

    the nuts. Do not harvest nuts for at least 30 days after application.

    </li>

</ul>

<p>

EC Emulsifiable concentrate</p>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Rhynchophorus ferrugineus</em>

Title: Sugarcane Woolly Aphids
Authors: Ms.    Girija
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    The sugarcane woolly aphid is a pest

that has profuse white coloured woolly coating around it. It is often mistaken

for a mealybug. The white powder on the

ground and leaves confirms the presence of the aphids. If uncontrolled,

it spreads very quickly and can cause yield losses of up to 20%.&nbsp;This pest is locally known as &ldquo;Panju asuvini&rdquo; (Tamil). The nymphs and adults of the aphid remain on the under surface of the leaves and suck the sap. The pests feed voraciously and convert excess sugar into honeydew. The honeydew secretion will lead to the growth of sooty mould fungus.
    &nbsp;The incidence of this pest starts immediately after rainy

season and becomes severe during summer and when there are prolonged dry spells

coupled with high relative humidity. The pest is transmitted by wind as well as

by the transportation of sett and cane from an affected field to other areas. The

pest survives on the ratoon crop as well as on hosts like grasses and banana

during the off season.
    <ul>

    <li>

    Remove

    and destroy affected plants and burn them, keeping them away from the field</li>

    <li>Completely

    avoid transport of affected sugarcane or seed material from the affected places

    to other areas</li>

    <li>Practice

    wide row/paired row planting @ 4 feet apart and detrash the cane for greater

    aeration and light penetration</li>

    <li>Avoid

    ratoon crop</li>

    <li>Release

    major predators such as <em>Dipha aphidivora</em>,

    <em>Micromus igorotus</em> and&nbsp;<em>Eupeodes confrater</em>&nbsp;<em></em> @ 1000 larvae or

    cocoons per ha as well as <em>Chrysopa</em>

    adults @ 12000 adults per acre</li>

    <li>Spray <em>Beauveria</em> / <em>Metarhizium </em>@

    1 ml / litre of water</li>

    <li>Treat

    sugarcane setts with malathion 50% EC @ 1 ml/litre of water for 15 minutes</li>

    <li>Spray azadirachtin (e.g.    Neemazal) 1500ppm @ 2ml/ litre or profenofos 50% EC @ 2ml/lit of water using a

    rocker sprayer </li>

</ul>

EC- Emulsifiable concentrate
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Ceratovacuna lanigera</em>
Images: 8782b9b2-159e-4235-a608-3baecffcf9cc.jpg, 8be8c774-ee76-4a51-90e1-5113b04a338a.jpg

Title: Bhendi Vein Clearing Virus
Authors: S.   Malarvannan, Vijay S Thokre
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    This is a viral disease occurring on bhendi (Okra/Lady&rsquo;s Finger). Yellowing of the entire network of veins in the leaf blade is the

characteristic symptom. In severe infections, the younger leaves turn yellow to

become entirely chlorotic and the plant is highly stunted. The veins of the

leaves will be cleared by the virus and interveinal area becomes completely

yellow or white. The veins become considerably

thickened. The infection may start at any stage of plant

growth. Infection restricts flowering and fruits, if formed, may be smaller and

harder. The affected plants produce fruits with yellow or white colour and they

are not fit for marketing.
    Bhendi vein clearing virus is a serious disease of Bhendi and causes huge

economic loss. The virus is spread by an insect

called a whitefly (<em>Bemisia tabaci</em>) that sucks the cell sap from the

underside of the leaves. The infection may start at any stage of plant growth
    <ul>

    <li>Grow resistant varieties like Parbhani Kranti, Arka Abhay, Arka

    Anamika and Varsha Uphar to minimise the incidence of the disease</li>

</ul>

<ul>

    <li>Maintain

    proper crop spacing</li>

</ul>

<ul>

    <li>Raise maize as a border crop to trap the insect thus preventing

    damage to main field and avoid transmittance of the disease</li>

</ul>

<ul>

    <li>During the summer season, when the whitefly activity is high, avoid

    growing susceptible varieties</li>

</ul>

<ul>

    <li>Set up yellow sticky traps @12/acre above the plant height to

    minimize the pest incidence</li>

</ul>

<ul>

    <li>Synthetic

    Pyrethroids should not be used because they will aggravate the situation</li>

</ul>

<ul>

    <li>When the infestation is 5-10 nymphs or adults per leaf before 9

    A.M. then go for chemical control:</li>

</ul>

<blockquote>

<ul>

</ul>

</blockquote>

<ul>

    <ul>

        <li>Spray acephate 75% SP @ 3 gm/ lit of

        water.</li>

    </ul>

</ul>

<ul>

    <ul>

        <li>Apply chlorpyrifos 20% EC @ 2.5 ml +

        Neem oil @ 2 ml/ litre of water</li>

    </ul>

</ul>

<blockquote>

</blockquote>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: Pathogen:&nbsp;Bhendi Yellow Vein Mosaic Virus, Insect vector: <em>Bemisia

tabaci</em>

Title: Tomato Yellow Leaf Curl Virus
Authors: R.   Ramesh, S.   Mathiyazhagan, K.   Rajappan, M.   Malarvannan
Editors: <?>
Sections:
    Tomato Yellow Leaf Curl Virus (TYLCV) is a
major problem on tomato, particularly in Tamil Nadu. Once the disease has
occurred, it will spread fast and drastically reduce the yield. Symptoms of
infection include stunted plants, reduced leaf size, upward cupping/curling
of leaves, chlorosis on leaves and reduced fruit production. Leaves are often
bent downward and may be thicker than normal leaves. Flowers may appear
normal but can sometimes bear no fruit, or, can bear fruit but significantly
reduced in size. Affected plants tend to be distributed in isolated patches.
The new growth of plants with TYLCV can have reduced internodes, giving the
plant a stunted appearance. This virus can cause significant yield losses
from 80-100%
    TYLCV is known as the most destructive disease of tomato
and can be found in tropical and subtropical regions, causing severe economic
losses. TYLCV
is transmitted through a vector and harboured inside infected plants; it is
not transmitted through seed or soil.&nbsp;
The disease is transmitted by white fly (<em>Bemisia tabaci</em>). The
primary host for TYLCV is the tomato plant. Other plant hosts of TYLCV
include eggplant, potato, tobacco, beans, and peppers. Due to the rapid
spread of TYLCV in the last few decades, there is an increased focus in
research trying to understand and control this disease.
    <ul>
    <li>
    Plant
    resistant variety Arka Ananya.</li>
    <li>Destroy
    old infested crop after harvest.
    </li>
    <li>Raise
    barrier crops &ndash; cereals (3 rows of sorghum, cumbu and maize) around the
    field.</li>
    <li>Plant
    marigold as border crop.
    </li>
    <li>Remove
    weeds as these may harbour the virus.
    </li>
    <li>Adopt
    polythene mulching technology during planting to control weed hosts.
    </li>
    <li>Physically
    protect the nursery from the vector, e.g. net house or green house.</li>
    <li>Keep
    yellow sticky traps @ 5/ac to monitor the white fly.
    </li>
    <li>Spray
    Imidacloprid 200 SL @ 0.4 ml / litre of water or Thimethoxam 25% WP @ 0.3 gram /
    lit or Acetamipride 20% SP @ 1 gram / lit or a combination of Acetamipride 20%
    SP @ 1 gram + Bifenthrin 10% EC @ 3.5 ml per lit or Diafeunthiuron 25% WP @ 2.5
    gram per lit at the interval of 15, 25 and 45 days after planting to control the vector. </li>
</ul>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Tomato yellow leaf curl virus</em>
Images: 12b929ed-131c-4aa7-822c-f0ca69654caa.jpg
Content:
Tomato Yellow Leaf Curl Virus (TYLCV) is a
major problem on tomato, particularly in Tamil Nadu. Once the disease has
occurred, it will spread fast and drastically reduce the yield. Symptoms of
infection include stunted plants, reduced leaf size, upward cupping/curling
of leaves, chlorosis on leaves and reduced fruit production. Leaves are often
bent downward and may be thicker than normal leaves. Flowers may appear
normal but can sometimes bear no fruit, or, can bear fruit but significantly
reduced in size. Affected plants tend to be distributed in isolated patches.
The new growth of plants with TYLCV can have reduced internodes, giving the
plant a stunted appearance. This virus can cause significant yield losses
from 80-100% TYLCV is known as the most destructive disease of tomato
and can be found in tropical and subtropical regions, causing severe economic
losses. TYLCV
is transmitted through a vector and harboured inside infected plants; it is
not transmitted through seed or soil.&nbsp;
The disease is transmitted by white fly (<em>Bemisia tabaci</em>). The
primary host for TYLCV is the tomato plant. Other plant hosts of TYLCV
include eggplant, potato, tobacco, beans, and peppers. Due to the rapid
spread of TYLCV in the last few decades, there is an increased focus in
research trying to understand and control this disease. <ul>
    <li>
    Plant
    resistant variety Arka Ananya.</li>
    <li>Destroy
    old infested crop after harvest.
    </li>
    <li>Raise
    barrier crops &ndash; cereals (3 rows of sorghum, cumbu and maize) around the
    field.</li>
    <li>Plant
    marigold as border crop.
    </li>
    <li>Remove
    weeds as these may harbour the virus.
    </li>
    <li>Adopt
    polythene mulching technology during planting to control weed hosts.
    </li>
    <li>Physically
    protect the nursery from the vector, e.g. net house or green house.</li>
    <li>Keep
    yellow sticky traps @ 5/ac to monitor the white fly.
    </li>
    <li>Spray
    Imidacloprid 200 SL @ 0.4 ml / litre of water or Thimethoxam 25% WP @ 0.3 gram /
    lit or Acetamipride 20% SP @ 1 gram / lit or a combination of Acetamipride 20%
    SP @ 1 gram + Bifenthrin 10% EC @ 3.5 ml per lit or Diafeunthiuron 25% WP @ 2.5
    gram per lit at the interval of 15, 25 and 45 days after planting to control the vector. </li>
</ul> When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.

Title: Chickpea Wilt
Authors: Vijay S Thokre
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    Chickpea wilt caused by <em>Fusarium oxysporum</em> f. sp. <em>ciceris</em> is one of the major yield limiting factors in chickpea and is also known as &ldquo;Mar disease&rdquo; in Marathi. The disease causes 10&ndash;90% yield losses annually in chickpea. When the disease occurs at seedling stage, seedlings collapse and lie flat on the soil surface. In adult plants, the characteristic symptom is a brown to black discoloration of the xylem vessels in the stem. Affected plants appear in patches in the field.
    The fungus may survive in seeds or in soil. The disease is more severe in light sandy soil than heavy clay. High soil temperature and lack of moisture appear to have a definite bearing on its incidence. The higher the organic matter in the soil, the lower the incidence of wilt. The development of wilt is favored by an increase in nitrogen. The optimum temperature and pH for the pathogen are 25&deg;C and pH 5-6.5 respectively. A delay in sowing helps to minimize the disease.
    <ul>

<li>Follow deep summer ploughing</li>

<li>Follow crop rotation measures continuously with unrelated crops e.g. wheat, barley or oats</li>

<li>Always use disease free seeds</li>

<li>Avoid sowing when temperature is high</li>

<li>Apply castor cake 500 kg/ha at the time of sowing</li>

<li>Treat seeds with <em>Trichoderma viride</em> @ 4 g/kg seed or with carbendazim @ 2 g/kg. Do not use both of these treatments as the chemical will kill the biological control agent.</li>

</ul>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Fusarium oxysporum</em> f.sp.<em> ciceris</em>
Images: 2b10f916-f606-4f20-bc83-eef3d09b0298.jpg
Content:

Title: Sugarcane Early shoot borer
Authors: T.   Aruna
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    The sugarcane early shoot borer is a serious problem in sugarcane. 1-3 month old crops are highly susceptible. The caterpillars of the shoot borer cause dead hearts in young plants. The young plants with dead hearts formed at shoot stage can be pulled out easily. The canes are damaged and also produce a foul odour. The central whorl of leaves dries up in the damaged plants.
    The symptoms described above are caused by an insect called a shoot borer. The adults, which are small pale greyish brown moths, lay white flat eggs in clusters of 8-60 eggs in 3 to 5 rows on the under surface of the leaf sheaths. The larvae, which hatch from the eggs in 1-6 days, get scattered and enter the stem by making a hole just above the ground level. It becomes fully grown in 25-30 days and pupates inside the stem. An adult moth emerges after 6-8 days. The total life cycle is completed in 35-40 days. The bore holes can be seen above the ground level.
    <ul>

<li>Destroy the crop residues after harvest</li>

<li>Plant early to avoid infestation</li>

<li>Intercrop with Daincha (Agathi)</li>

<li>Remove and destroy the dried shoots</li>

<li>Complete inundative releases of the egg parasitoid <em>Trichogramma</em> chilonis @ 50,000/ha from the first month of planting at 7-10 day intervals till one month prior to harvest</li>

<li>At the 30th, 45th and 60th day of crop growth, spray granulosis virus of sugarcane shoot borer (<em>Chilo infuscatellus</em>) (8-10 inclusion bodies/ml) at 500 litres/ha</li>

<li>Soil application of granules of cartap hydrochloride at 1 kg a.i./ha at planting followed by another application on 45th day for late planted crop</li>

<li>Cartap hydrochloride 4G and chlorpyriphos 10G at 1 kg a.i./ha as a whorl application at 35th and 65th day are also found to be effective</li>

</ul>

a.i. &ndash; active ingredient
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Chilo infuscatellus</em>
Images: 3636a026-9e03-4762-b66d-6345e656821d.jpg, fa1b9c92-c968-402a-86cc-0bc504200a3d.jpg

Title: Pulse Beetle
Authors: Vijay S Thokre
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    Pulse beetles are serious pests of stored pulses like pigeon pea, green gram, black gram, cowpea and chickpea. The white coloured eggs hatch after 4 to 5 days. The grub makes a hole inside the grain which causes heavy damage. This reduces the quality as well as market prices. Adults are 4.5 mm long and heart shaped.
    This is a primary pest of stored pulses. Infestation starts in the field and continues in the store. The grubs penetrate grains and feed on the inner content. Infested grains are unfit for human consumption and cannot be used as seeds. Maximum damage occurs when conditions are favourable i.e. an optimum temperature of 30&deg;C and relative humidity of 70%. This pest completes its life cycle in 25 to 40 days and causes heavy yield losses. The pest can be effectively managed using various physical methods.
    All the stages of pulse beetle in seeds could be killed by exposing them to temperatures of up to 45&deg;C for varying periods, or by exposing them to sunlight for 4 to 6 hours with the diurnal temperature varying from 37 - 45&deg;C. Viability of the treated grains not will be adversely affected. <br />

<ul>

<li>Clean the warehouses and storage places with brushes, scrapers or a vacuum cleaner, then destroy any collected insect</li>

</ul>

<p>For storing the grains, use any of the following methods:</p>

<ul>

<li>Mix ash with grains</li>

<li>Use neem leaves at @ 1 kg / 100g of grains</li>

<li>Apply neem oil @ 8-10ml / kg seed, fenvalerate 0.4% @ 4 gm/kg seed, malathion 50EC @ 0.06ml/kg seed or deltamethrin 2.8 EC (0.04ml)/ kg for seed treatment</li>

<li>Dust with 5% malathion</li>

</ul>

<p>&nbsp;</p>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Callosobruchus</em> spp.
Images: 99f40849-6aee-4595-a23b-6a809f226e97.jpg, c954c970-5358-4bbe-855d-da22c409e1bb.jpg

<p>&nbsp;</p> When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.

Title: Cotton Mealybug Management
Authors: Bhavana   Kolhe
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    The cotton mealybug is a sucking pest of cotton and sucks the cell sap from the leaves, fruit and twigs. Serious attacks result in delayed growth and late opening of bolls which severely affects yield. It feeds on soft tissues and injects saliva, which causes curling of the leaves. Mealybugs secrete a sugary substance on which black sooty mould growth is observed. This also attracts ant colonies.
    The mealybug body is covered with very short waxy filaments. It spreads from the borders of the field. It is a polyphagous pest and feeds on various crops and weeds, etc. In initial stages, it can be observed in small pockets. More damage occurs during dry weather conditions. The main vector of their transmission is ants but they also get transmitted by humans, water, air, etc. Therefore, controlling ants will allow predators to feed on mealybugs, reducing their numbers.
    <ul>

<li>Use crop rotation practices and burn the plant debris to maintain the field sanitation</li>

<li>Remove or avoid growing the alternate host plants of mealybug such as <em>Parthenium</em> spp., datura, milkweed, <em>Chenopodium</em> spp., okra, tomato and brinjal near cotton fields</li>

<li>Remove and burn the affected plant parts</li>

<li>Encourage the activity of predators like <em>Cryptolaemus montrouzieri</em> and <em>Cheilomenes sexmaculata</em>, commonly known as ladybird beetles</li>

<li>Locate the ant colonies and destroy them by drenching with chlorpyriphos 50EC @ 2.5 ml/ litres of water</li>

<li>If the infestation continues, use any one insecticide and add detergent powder 20gm / 10 lit of water for the degradation of the waxy coating</li>

<li>After harvesting, store cotton sticks away from the fields and if possible a barrier of insecticide (e.g. malathion dusting) should be made around the stacks to reduce the carry over to the next crop</li>

</ul>

<p>EC- Emulsifiable concentrate</p>
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Phenacoccus solenopsis</em>
Images: 2726201f-9a2e-4033-b810-c2958ae78847.jpg, 42bd0758-2b36-4d04-a193-18c039ef9fa3.jpg

Title: Yellow stem borer on paddy
Authors: C  Chandramohan, Ramana  Murthy, S R Ingle
Editors: Eric
Sections:
    Yellow stem borer is the most damaging insect on paddy. It feeds inside the plant. Stems become dry and hollow and no grains are produced. The dried stems can be pulled out easily and are called dead hearts. The empty grains are known as white ears and detach easily.
    The insect has two different forms. The adult insect is usually a yellowish moth with a black dot on each wing and has a pointed mouth. The adults visit the fields in the evening. The young larvae or worms are found inside the stem and are off white with a red head and no visible legs.<br /> The larva is thin with a rough surface and looks like a small tube. They cause much damage by boring into the stem and eating the plant. Only one larva is found in each stem although several stems can be affected.
    There are no resistant varieties of rice and chemical sprays are not effective once the larvae are hidden inside in the stem.<br /> Early treatment of young plants helps to reduce damage in fields. In raised beds of seedlings spread <em>Cartap HCL </em>10G evenly. This is an approved granular insecticide that dissolves in water and remains within the plant. A raised bed of one foot by 10 feet will need around 13 grams of<em> Cartap HCL 10G</em>.<br /> After transplanting seedlings, and within 10 days, place pheromone dispensers containing sex pheromone tablets in the paddy field. These will attract adult moths and reduce populations of larvae. Place 32 dispensers per acre, evenly spaced. These should remain in the field till harvest. There is no need to replace the tablets during this time.
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Scirpophaga incertulas</em>
Images: 19FFAB59-1AE1-49FB-93C7-7D0D123DB58B.jpg, 8BFBF6DE-83EB-4712-ABFE-065F2B34C030.jpg, 17977831-5D59-4C46-B49E-1A2D62097F2B.jpg

Title: Cabbage aphid
Authors: R.  Manjesh
Editors: Eric
Sections:
    Cabbage aphids are small insects which damage plants at all stages and reduce the value of the crop. Locally they are called <em>beli henu</em>. They are dull green with a white, waxy surface and often occur in dense groups. They are easy to identify.
    Cabbage aphids feed on both sides of young and tender leaves. They breed very quickly and populations increase rapidly during hot and humid conditions.<br /> Large numbers of aphids can kill seedlings. On older plants, leaves curl and become yellow as they die, plant growth is stunted and cabbage heads are deformed.
    Management options include prevention and control.<br /> PREVENTION: check the seedlings regularly for aphids. If the seedling is heavily infested do not use as transplants.<br /> NATURE'S CONTROL: Less use of insecticides helps to maintain a healthy balance of helpful insects which feed on aphids.<br /> ORGANIC CONTROL: Treat early to keep aphids in check. Insecticidal soaps such as LASTRAW are applied at 5 millilitres per litre of water. Spray two times each week for three weeks when the pest is present in the field<br /> CHEMICAL CONTROL: If the pest still persists spray with insecticides such as Acetamiprid, Chloropyriphos and Imidacloprid, at recommended doses. Granular insecticides can be applied to soil during transplanting of seedlings.
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Brevicoryne brassicae</em>
Images: AB5D0AD4-8716-4AE7-BC2C-FA163E96380F.jpg, 89418473-9409-406C-B454-ED848F89DE1E.jpg
Content:
Cabbage aphids are small insects which damage plants at all stages and reduce the value of the crop. Locally they are called <em>beli henu</em>. They are dull green with a white, waxy surface and often occur in dense groups. They are easy to identify. Cabbage aphids feed on both sides of young and tender leaves. They breed very quickly and populations increase rapidly during hot and humid conditions.<br /> Large numbers of aphids can kill seedlings. On older plants, leaves curl and become yellow as they die, plant growth is stunted and cabbage heads are deformed. Management options include prevention and control.<br /> PREVENTION: check the seedlings regularly for aphids. If the seedling is heavily infested do not use as transplants.<br /> NATURE'S CONTROL: Less use of insecticides helps to maintain a healthy balance of helpful insects which feed on aphids.<br /> ORGANIC CONTROL: Treat early to keep aphids in check. Insecticidal soaps such as LASTRAW are applied at 5 millilitres per litre of water. Spray two times each week for three weeks when the pest is present in the field<br /> CHEMICAL CONTROL: If the pest still persists spray with insecticides such as Acetamiprid, Chloropyriphos and Imidacloprid, at recommended doses. Granular insecticides can be applied to soil during transplanting of seedlings. When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.

Title: Leaf curl disease on tomato
Authors: S. K Ghosh, V. S Mahesh
Editors: Eric
Sections:
    Leaf curl is a virus disease which reduces yields severely. The disease can be confused with other problems such as tomato yellow top disease, low temperature damage and poor soils. The distinctive feature of leaf curl disease apart from the curling is severe stunting of plants, particularly in seedlings.
    The disease is caused by a germ (virus) and is spread by a small sucking insect called whitefly. The whiteflies inject the germ into healthy plants. The whiteflies occur on the underside of the leaf and fly away when the plant is disturbed.<br /> The virus is not transmitted in seed, soil or by handling plants. Weeds can carry the virus but they do not show any symptoms.<br /> Diseased plants grow slowly, have smaller yellow leaves, are stunted and produce less fruit. The edges of leaves curl unevenly in different directions and have a rough surface. The flowers and fruits appear healthy. Infected seedlings have bushy growth.
    There is no chemical control for leaf curl disease. There are other methods which help control the spread of whiteflies and the disease.

<ul>

<li>Raise the seedlings under protective netting (less than 50 mesh size) supported by small wooden sticks.</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>Control weeds in fields by covering the soil with paddy straw or similar material or use plastic sheeting.</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>LASTRAW is an insecticidal soap which can help control whiteflies. Spray twice a week for three weeks.</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>At the early stages of disease development, when only a few plants show leaf curl symptoms, remove and burn.</li>

</ul>

Tomato varieties tolerant to leaf curl disease are available but are not currently sold in India.
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: Tomato yellow leaf curl virus
Images: 4B200743-8068-4427-BD70-10BDE2257495.jpg, 89121BEF-D446-4F1D-8DF2-5ADE40D59B57.jpg

Title: Root knot nematode on tomato
Authors: Anusha  Babbar, Malvika  Chaudhary, Rekha  Powar
Editors: Eric
Sections:
    Tomato roots are attacked by nematodes, reducing growth of the plant and yields. The symptoms above ground are similar to other diseases. Confirm nematode attack by examining the roots.
    Nematodes or <em>Jantu hula</em> are small worms which live in the soil, and cannot be seen with the naked eye. They can persist for many years, even after the infected plant has been removed. Nursery soil is a common source of nematodes.<br /> How to recognize tomato nematode:

<ul>

<li>distinctive swellings or knots on the roots</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>the plant is stunted (it cannot absorb enough food)</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>leaves go yellow and die</li>

</ul>

Nematodes attack many plants. Knots are often confused with nodules found on the roots of crops such as cowpea and bean. These nodules help the plant to grow.
    <em>Pseudomonas fluorescens</em> is a natural control method. SU-MONAS is one example of a commercial preparation. When applied to the soil it helps tomato plants fight nematodes. Treat nursery soil to prevent transfer of nematodes to fields.

<ul>

<li>In the nursery apply SU-MONAS at 50 grams per square metre of soil.</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>In case of infected seedlings, broadcast the field with well decomposed farmyard manure (1 ton per acre) enriched by SU-MONAS at 2 kilograms per 100 kilograms of farmyard manure. Apply this treatment before you transplant the seedlings.</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>Intercrop every 25 rows of tomato with one row of marigold. Their root juices kill nematodes.</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>Ensure good drainage after irrigation by keeping the ridge height of the seedling bed up to 8-10 inches.</li>

</ul>

<ul>

<li>Do not plant tomatoes continuously. Rotate every six months with fodder crops such as sorghum which are not attacked by nematodes.</li>

</ul>

Chemical control of nematodes in the soil is available but expensive.
    When using a pesticide, always wear protective clothing and follow the instructions on the product label, such as dosage, timing of application, and pre-harvest interval.
Scientific Names: <em>Meloidogynes spp.</em>
Images: 74615861-0A28-4637-992F-306D363D6444.jpg, 614F3DF3-88F7-4587-8962-C69D5FAC91FC.jpg

Title: Irrigation Quality
Authors: S. G Matale
Editors: CABI
Sections:
    Irrigation with impure or poor quality water will have a negative effect on the soil properties and, ultimately, on crop fertility and yield. The constitution and concentration of salts in water can have negative effect on soil.
    Farmers use irrigation water without following the scientific guidelines. Total salt concentration is the most important standard for judging the irrigation quality. Irrigation scheduling should be based on the water requirement of the crop.
    The suitability of irrigation water is based on the classification and concentration of salts in water. <br /> <br /> C1 low salinity - suitable for most soil <br /> C2 medium salinity - suitable with moderate drainage <br /> C3 high salinity - unsuitable under restricted condition <br /> C4 very high salinity - unsuitable<br /> <br />

<ul>

<li>Drain excess water from the field</li>

<li>Use gypsum (to measure soil moisture) along with land treatments</li>

<li>Use green manures and crop residues to develop the physical properties of soil</li>

</ul>
Scientific Names: 
Images: 2e16ec73-9c60-406d-95a1-3b2822b0f795.jpg
Content:

<li>Drain excess water from the field</li>

<li>Use gypsum (to measure soil moisture) along with land treatments</li>

<li>Use green manures and crop residues to develop the physical properties of soil</li>

</ul>

Title: Banana Pseudostem Weevil
Authors:   Dr. T. Elaiyabharathi (TNAU), Dr. M.Asokhan (TNAU), T. Aruna (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Remove dried leaves periodically and keep the plantation clean.<br/>-&nbsp;Prune the suckers every month.<br/>-&nbsp;Do not dump infested materials in the manure pit.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid using suckers for planting from affected areas<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting Nenthran banana variety which is highly susceptible<br/>
    -&nbsp;Look for presence of small pinhead-sized holes on the stem.<br/>-&nbsp;When pinholes are first detected, take action.<br/>-&nbsp;Other signs of infestation include the following: Jelly exudation on the stem is the initial symptom of damage. Due to secondary infection of pathogens, rotting occurs and a foul odour is emitted.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Trap the adult weevils with pseudostem chopped into small pieces.<br/>-&nbsp;Infested trees should be uprooted, chopped into pieces and burnt.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Carbaryl at 20grams per plant in the soil around the stem.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (moderately hazardous). Carbamate pesticide with contact and stomach action.<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Odoiporus longicollis</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_88219.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_88222.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_88223.img?width=800&height=640

Title: Button Shedding on Coconut
Authors:   Dr. T. Elaiyabharathi (TNAU), Dr. M. Asokhan (TNAU), T. Aruna (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Rectification of soil pH by adding gypsum.<br/>-&nbsp;Application of 0.5 ml of NAA (Planofix) mixed in 1 litre of water on the inflorescence. This prevents button shedding.<br/>-&nbsp;Soil mulching with green fronds reduces evapotranspiration and conserves soil moisture.<br/>-&nbsp;Ensuring sufficient irrigation during summer and drainage during winter will lead to a sustainable yield.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Look for shedding of buttons and premature nuts.<br/>-&nbsp;Look for contributing factors: Excess acidity or alkalinity in soil. Poor drainage facility. Severe and prolonged drought. Observe for presence of insect pests/diseases <br/>
    -&nbsp;Soil application of 1.3 Kg urea, 2 Kg of Single Super Phosphate and 2 Kg of potash mixed with 50 Kg of farm yard manure per palm with borax 150 gram per tree.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding of coconut tonic at 40 ml in 160 ml of water per palm once in 6 months will control the button shedding and enhance the yield.<br/>
    -&nbsp;No separate chemical management is available.<br/>
Scientific Names: Multiple causes
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_88401.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Rectification of soil pH by adding gypsum.<br/>-&nbsp;Application of 0.5 ml of NAA (Planofix) mixed in 1 litre of water on the inflorescence. This prevents button shedding.<br/>-&nbsp;Soil mulching with green fronds reduces evapotranspiration and conserves soil moisture.<br/>-&nbsp;Ensuring sufficient irrigation during summer and drainage during winter will lead to a sustainable yield.<br/> -&nbsp;Look for shedding of buttons and premature nuts.<br/>-&nbsp;Look for contributing factors: Excess acidity or alkalinity in soil. Poor drainage facility. Severe and prolonged drought. Observe for presence of insect pests/diseases <br/> -&nbsp;Soil application of 1.3 Kg urea, 2 Kg of Single Super Phosphate and 2 Kg of potash mixed with 50 Kg of farm yard manure per palm with borax 150 gram per tree.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding of coconut tonic at 40 ml in 160 ml of water per palm once in 6 months will control the button shedding and enhance the yield.<br/> -&nbsp;No separate chemical management is available.<br/>

Title: Rhinoceros Beetle
Authors:   Dr. T. Elaiyabharathi (TNAU), Dr. M. Asokhan (TNAU), T. Aruna (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Cleaning of the crown region before the onset of monsoons.<br/>-&nbsp;Periodical raking of manure pit to expose larvae to predation.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect and destroy the various biostages of the beetle from the manure pits.<br/>-&nbsp;Incorporate the entomopathogenic fungus <i>Metarhizium anisoplae</i> in manure pits to check the perpetuation of the pest. <br/>
    -&nbsp;Set up light traps following the first rains in summer and monsoon period to attract the adult beetles.<br/>-&nbsp;Set up Rhinolure pheromone trap at 2 traps per  ha to monitor adults.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Set up light traps following the first rains in summer and monsoon period to attract and kill the adult beetles.<br/>-&nbsp;Set up Rhinolure pheromone trap @ 2/2 ha to trap and kill the beetles.<br/>-&nbsp;Soak castor (<i>Ricinus communis</i>) cake in small mud pots and keep them in the coconut (<i>Cocos nucifera</i>) garden.<br/>-&nbsp;Longitudinally split tender coconut stem or fronds with fresh toddy or vinegar and keep them in the garden. Collect and destroy the congregated beetles.<br/>-&nbsp;Spread neem seed powder plus sand in equal proportion on the crown region.<br/>-&nbsp;Trap adult beetles by keeping containers with curd + yeast + banana paste in small sachet.<br/>-&nbsp;Hook out and kill the adults at the crown region regularly.<br/>-&nbsp;For seedlings, apply 3 nos. of naphthalene balls/palm weighing 3.5 g each at the base of interspace of leaf sheath. <br/>-&nbsp;Field release of <i>Baculovirus oryctes</i> inoculated adult rhinoceros beetle reduces the leaf and crown damage caused by this beetle.  <br/>
    -&nbsp;Carbosulfan. Carbamate. Systemic with contact and stomach action

.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Highly toxic to aquatic organisms, bees, birds and mammals<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Oryctes rhinoceros</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89062.img?width=800&height=640

Title: Rice Leaf Folder
Authors:   Mr. Vijay Thokre, Mr. Nilesh Patkar, Dr. Malarvanan & Mr Rajkumar
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Summer ploughing, trimming of bunds and destruction of crop residues.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid excessive use of nitrogenous fertilizers, Avoid close planting; leave 30 cm space at every 2.5 m.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use light traps at 12 places in paddy field in between 6 to 9 pm to monitor pest incidence.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Release <i>Trichogramma chilonis</i> thrice at 3 cc per acre.<br/>-&nbsp;Spread neem leaves randomly in the field to deter the adults from egg laying.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray of neem oil 300 ml + kerosene 250 ml + 50 g khadi soap will be very effective.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray of 300 ml neem oil + 300 ml pongamia oil + 50 grams khadi soap will afford protection.<br/>-&nbsp;Place bamboo tops or twigs for perching of predatory birds soon after planting. However, these are to be removed at flowering stage of the crop.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spraying of quinalphos 25 EC @ 25 ml / 10 litre water (Ekalux). Organophosphate. Broad spectrum contact and stomach poison.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (Moderately hazardous)<br/>-&nbsp;Spraying of profenofos 50 EC @ 10 ml / 10 litre water. Organophosphate. Broad spectrum insecticide with contact and stomach action.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (Moderately hazardous)<br/>

Title: Spotted Pod Borer on Pigeon Pea
Authors:   Dr. V.R. Saminathan (TNAU), Mr. G. Sudakar (MSSRF), Dr. K. Chozhan (TNAU), Ms. D.S. Girija (MSSRF).
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Optimum level of nitrogenous fertilizer as per soil testing recommendation.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid closer sowing for erect varieties such as APK1.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting Sunn hemp in the borders as it is an alternate host.<br/>-&nbsp;Follow crop rotation with non-host crops such as paddy, maize, etc.<br/>-&nbsp;Grow resistant varieties of pigeon pea e.g., ICPL-87119.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor the pest by setting up light traps @ one per five hectare.<br/>-&nbsp;Look for presence of bore holes on the buds, flowers and pods. <br/>-&nbsp;Look for webbing of leaves, flowers and pods.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Erection of bird perches @ 15 numbers per hectare.<br/>-&nbsp;Application of azadirachtin 3 ml per litre of water at the onset of pest attack.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Apply insecticides in rotation on need basis to protect the pollinators since red gram is a highly cross pollinated crop.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Emamectin benzoate 220 gm/ha. Avermectin insecticide. Systemic contact and stomach poison.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Not classified by WHO. Highly toxic to aquatic organisms, bees, birds and mammals<br/>-&nbsp;Application of <i>Bacillus thuringiensis</i> @ 1 kg/ha.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class III (Slightly hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Spray spinosad 45% SC @ 125ml/ha.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class III (Slightly hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Chlorantraniliprole 18.5% SC @ 150ml/ha. Diamide insecticide. Systemic insecticide with contact and stomach action.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class U (Unlikely to present acute hazard in normal use).<br/>-&nbsp;Flubendiamide 480 SC @ 75 a.i/ha. Diamide insecticide. Locally systemic with translaminar activity. Contact and stomach action.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Not classified by WHO; classified as slightly hazardous by the EPA<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Maruca testulalis</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89229.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89232.img?width=800&height=640

Title: Tea Mosquito Bug on Cashew
Authors:   Dr. V.R. Saminathan (TNAU), Mr. G. Sudakar (MSSRF), Dr. K. Chozhan (TNAU), Ms. D.S.Girija (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Use of tolerant variety such as VRI2<br/>-&nbsp;Remove the neem trees in the fence and in the nearby area.<br/>-&nbsp;Apply recommended level of nitrogenous fertilizer based on the soil test report<br/>-&nbsp;Do not use guava as an inter-crop.<br/>-&nbsp;Proper pruning to get maximum sunlight inside the canopy<br/>
    -&nbsp;Carry out regular observation for infestation during the month of September to April.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe for any oozing out, necrosis symptoms.<br/>-&nbsp;If the damage is more than 1%, go for control measures.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray <i>Beauveria bassiana</i> at 400g/acre at an interval of fifteen days (three times).<br/>-&nbsp;Release of <i>Chrysoperla carnea</i> at 25,000 eggs per acre once.<br/>-&nbsp;Spraying of Neem Oil 2%. <br/>
    -&nbsp;As cashew is a cross pollinated crop, the dosage should not be increased in order to avoid toxicity to pollinators.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid spraying during mid-day as the bugs may hide under dense canopy.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Profenofos 50% EC @ 2ml/litre of water during October to November.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Organophosphate. Non-systemic with stomach and contact action.<br/>-&nbsp;Chlorpyrifos 50 EC 2ml/litre during November to December.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II. Organophosphate. Non-systemic insecticide with contact, stomach and respiratory action.<br/>-&nbsp;Carbaryl 50 WP 3g/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II. Carbamate. Contact and stomach action with slight systemic properties.<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Helopeltis antonii</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89289.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89292.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89293.img?width=800&height=640

-&nbsp;Use of tolerant variety such as VRI2<br/>-&nbsp;Remove the neem trees in the fence and in the nearby area.<br/>-&nbsp;Apply recommended level of nitrogenous fertilizer based on the soil test report<br/>-&nbsp;Do not use guava as an inter-crop.<br/>-&nbsp;Proper pruning to get maximum sunlight inside the canopy<br/> -&nbsp;Carry out regular observation for infestation during the month of September to April.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe for any oozing out, necrosis symptoms.<br/>-&nbsp;If the damage is more than 1%, go for control measures.<br/> -&nbsp;Spray <i>Beauveria bassiana</i> at 400g/acre at an interval of fifteen days (three times).<br/>-&nbsp;Release of <i>Chrysoperla carnea</i> at 25,000 eggs per acre once.<br/>-&nbsp;Spraying of Neem Oil 2%. <br/> -&nbsp;As cashew is a cross pollinated crop, the dosage should not be increased in order to avoid toxicity to pollinators.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid spraying during mid-day as the bugs may hide under dense canopy.<br/> -&nbsp;Profenofos 50% EC @ 2ml/litre of water during October to November.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Title: Blast in Paddy
Authors:   Mr. G. Sudhakar (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Decompose the straw and stubbles after the harvest.<br/>-&nbsp;Use a tolerant variety such as CO 47, CO 50, ADT 36, ADT 37, ASD 16, ASD 20, ADT 39,ASD 19,TPS 3,White ponni, ADT 44, CORH, Palghuna, Swarnamukhi, Swathi, Prabhat, IR-64 and  IR  36.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting of susceptible varieties like BPT-5204.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid dry nursery management. <br/>-&nbsp;Avoid late season planting (November month).<br/>-&nbsp;Apply recommended level of Nitrogenous fertilizer in three split doses 50% basal, 25% in tillering and 25% in panicle initiation stage. Avoid excess application of Nitrogen fertilizer.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Cloudy skies, frequent rain, drizzles, high relative humidity (90% and higher) and temperatures of 25-28C<br/>-&nbsp;Carry out regular observation from nursery to active tillering stage. <br/>-&nbsp;Initial symptoms are white to gray-green lesions or spots with brown borders.<br/>-&nbsp;Small specks originate on leaves surface -subsequently enlarge into spindle shaped spots with ashy center.  <br/>-&nbsp;It affects all the parts leaf, neck and nodal portions.<br/>-&nbsp;5% ETL disease severity noticed in the field.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Seed treatment with <i>Pseudomonas fluorescens</i> talc formulation @10g/kg of seed. <br/>-&nbsp;Root dipping of seedlings in <i>Pseudomonas fluorescens</i> @ 2.5kg, dipped for 30 minutes. <br/>-&nbsp;Spray <i>Pseudomonas fluorescens</i> talc formulation @ 10gm/ litre of water from 45 days after transplanting at 10 days intervals, three times of application. <br/>
    -&nbsp;Avoid spraying during the neck stage of crop damage.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Kasugamycin 3% SL @ 3.5ml/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class U (IV) (Unlikely to present acute hazard), Antibiotic, Systemic with translocation action.<br/>-&nbsp;Azoxystrobin 23% SC @ 2 ml/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class U (IV) (Unlikely to present acute hazard), Methoxyacrylate Strobilurin, Systemic fungicide.<br/>-&nbsp;Tebuconazole 25.9% m/m EC @ 2ml/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous), Conazole (triazole group), Systemic fungicide.<br/>-&nbsp;Tricyclazole 75% WP @ 2.5gm/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II, Chemical group unclassified, Systemic fungicide<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Pyricularia oryzae</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89488.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89490.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89491.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Decompose the straw and stubbles after the harvest.<br/>-&nbsp;Use a tolerant variety such as CO 47, CO 50, ADT 36, ADT 37, ASD 16, ASD 20, ADT 39,ASD 19,TPS 3,White ponni, ADT 44, CORH, Palghuna, Swarnamukhi, Swathi, Prabhat, IR-64 and  IR  36.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting of susceptible varieties like BPT-5204.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid dry nursery management. <br/>-&nbsp;Avoid late season planting (November month).<br/>-&nbsp;Apply recommended level of Nitrogenous fertilizer in three split doses 50% basal, 25% in tillering and 25% in panicle initiation stage. Title: Coconut Red Palm Weevil
Authors:   Mr. Rajkumar, Ms. D.S. Girija (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Avoid cutting of green fronds.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid wounding the trunk portion.<br/>-&nbsp;Removal and destruction of fully affected palms.<br/>-&nbsp;Effective management of Rhinoceros beetle (<i>Oryctes rhinoceros</i>) can prevent the secondary infection of red palm weevil.<br/>-&nbsp;Maintain field sanitation and regular cleaning of crowns.<br/>-&nbsp;Adult beetles can be collected and killed<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitoring the movement of adult beetles after the monsoon by installing light traps.<br/>-&nbsp;Oozing out of red brownish liquid from the entry hole of beetle. <br/>-&nbsp;Protruding out of chewed fibres in the bottom portion of palm.<br/>-&nbsp;In severe cases, wilting and bending of crown portion of palm is noticed.<br/>-&nbsp;Observing of gnawing sound of feeding grub during evening hours.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Place a mud pot attractant trap containing bits of tender stem, 2.5 litres of sugarcane molasses or pineapple pieces and 5 ml of vinegar or acetic acid at 5 traps per acre.<br/>-&nbsp;Installing pheromone traps @ one trap per 2.5 acres. Suspension of Ferrolure (4-methyl-5-nonanol) in one litre of water in the specially made coconut fibre wounded bucket along with 100 ml of sugarcane juice, 2 grams of yeast and two grams of carbaryl.<br/>-&nbsp;Replace the fluid once in the week to avoid mosquito breeding.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Stem treatment: 1. Sealing of all bore holes on the trunk leaving the topmost one. 2. Pouring of Carbaryl suspension at 20 grams per litre in the bore hole by using funnel and plug with red sand or cement. Stem treatment should be done after harvesting only.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Forty days should be allowed for next harvesting after treating the palm with stem treatment or root feeding. WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Carbamate. Contact and stomach action with slight systemic properties.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding: Sharply cut a fresh and live root slanting and feed the cut end with 10 ml of Profenofos 50% EC along with 10 ml of water in a polythene bag. Seal the bag with cotton thread. Absorption should be checked within a day.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;The control measures are ineffective if a wider tunnel is noticed in the trunk portion. WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Organophosphate. Non-systemic with stomach and contact action.<br/>-&nbsp;Carbaryl 50 WP 3grams/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Carbamate. Contact and stomach action with slight systemic properties<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Rhynchophorus ferrugineus</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89492.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89494.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89495.img?width=800&height=640
Content:

Title: Eriophyid Mite in Coconut
Authors:   G. Sudhakar (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Adopting proper phytosanitary measures and cleaning of palm crown in regular intervals.<br/>-&nbsp;Removal and burning of the fallen immature, mite infested nuts.<br/>-&nbsp;Provide adequate irrigation during summer months at 70 litres/day/palm.<br/>-&nbsp;Conserve the palm base soil moisture by mulching with coconut husk.<br/>-&nbsp;Follow the recommended N, P nutrients along with 3.5 kg of Potash/palm/year.<br/>-&nbsp;Application of nutrients in 3 equal splits in two month intervals i.e., July, September and January.<br/>-&nbsp;Planting of Casuarina along the borders of coconut garden to prevent the aerial entry of Eriophyid mites.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Careful observation of fallen nuts during the months of January to June for symptoms of eriophyid mites.<br/>-&nbsp;Monitoring the nuts up to 3rd month since they are more susceptible to this pest.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Sowing of green manure crops such as dhaincha (<i>Sesbania bispinosa</i>) or sunn hemp (<i>Crotalaria juncea</i>) at 30 grams/palm at the trunk base and in situ ploughing before flowering of the green manure crops.<br/>-&nbsp;Frond spraying of concoction made up of 400grams garlic + 400ml neem oil + 100 litres of water at 1 litre/palm during inflorescence and nut formation stage.<br/>-&nbsp;Prepare a spray solution of 20ml neem oil + 20 grams garlic paste 5 grams of soap bar dissolved in a litre of water and spray on the bunch.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding of botanical biocide at 40ml/palm.<br/>
    -&nbsp;After root feeding, next harvest should be done 45 days later.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding should be limited to young roots/palm.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Root feeding of Carbosulfan 25% EC 15ml + 15ml of water after every harvest from the months of January to June.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Carbamates group. Systemic.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding of Profenofos 50% EC 15ml + 15ml of water after every harvest from the months of January to June.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Phenyl organothiophosphate group. Systemic.<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Aceria guerreronis</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89508.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89510.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Adopting proper phytosanitary measures and cleaning of palm crown in regular intervals.<br/>-&nbsp;Removal and burning of the fallen immature, mite infested nuts.<br/>-&nbsp;Provide adequate irrigation during summer months at 70 litres/day/palm.<br/>-&nbsp;Conserve the palm base soil moisture by mulching with coconut husk.<br/>-&nbsp;Follow the recommended N, P nutrients along with 3.5 kg of Potash/palm/year.<br/>-&nbsp;Application of nutrients in 3 equal splits in two month intervals i.e., July, September and January.<br/>-&nbsp;Planting of Casuarina along the borders of coconut garden to prevent the aerial entry of Eriophyid mites.<br/> -&nbsp;Careful observation of fallen nuts during the months of January to June for symptoms of eriophyid mites.<br/>-&nbsp;Monitoring the nuts up to 3rd month since they are more susceptible to this pest.<br/> -&nbsp;Sowing of green manure crops such as dhaincha (<i>Sesbania bispinosa</i>) or sunn hemp (<i>Crotalaria juncea</i>) at 30 grams/palm at the trunk base and in situ ploughing before flowering of the green manure crops.<br/>-&nbsp;Frond spraying of concoction made up of 400grams garlic + 400ml neem oil + 100 litres of water at 1 litre/palm during inflorescence and nut formation stage.<br/>-&nbsp;Prepare a spray solution of 20ml neem oil + 20 grams garlic paste 5 grams of soap bar dissolved in a litre of water and spray on the bunch.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding of botanical biocide at 40ml/palm.<br/> -&nbsp;After root feeding, next harvest should be done 45 days later.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding should be limited to young roots/palm.<br/> -&nbsp;Root feeding of Carbosulfan 25% EC 15ml + 15ml of water after every harvest from the months of January to June.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Carbamates group. Systemic.<br/>-&nbsp;Root feeding of Profenofos 50% EC 15ml + 15ml of water after every harvest from the months of January to June.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Phenyl organothiophosphate group. Systemic.<br/>

Title: Gram Pod Borer
Authors:   Nilesh Patkar
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Deep ploughing at summer season.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid mono cropping.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove the plant debris from the field.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove the weeds from field<br/>
    -&nbsp;Installation of pheromone traps @5 nos./ha coinciding with the initiation of flowering and destruction of collected moths. <br/>-&nbsp;Field infestation 3 to 4 larvae per meter row length<br/>
    -&nbsp;Application of neem seed kernel extract 5% as a repellent for egg laying. <br/>-&nbsp;Application spray of NPV (nuclear polyhedrosis virus) @250 LE*/ha. (*LE = Larval Equivalent)<br/>-&nbsp;Various entomopathogenic fungi such as <i>Beauveria bassiana</i> and <i>Metarhizium anisopliae</i> @ 30 gram/10 liter have been effectively used to control lepidopteran larvae and other pests.<br/>-&nbsp;Release of the egg parasitoid, <i>Trichogramma chilonis</i> or <i>T. brasiliensis</i> @ 1 Lakh/ha coinciding with flower initiation at 15 days interval.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray fenvalerate 20 EC @ 10 ml/10 liter.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous)<br/>-&nbsp;Spray Profenofos 50EC @ 15ml/10 liter.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous)<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Helicoverpa armigera</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89516.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89519.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Deep ploughing at summer season.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid mono cropping.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove the plant debris from the field.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove the weeds from field<br/> -&nbsp;Installation of pheromone traps @5 nos./ha coinciding with the initiation of flowering and destruction of collected moths. <br/>-&nbsp;Field infestation 3 to 4 larvae per meter row length<br/> -&nbsp;Application of neem seed kernel extract 5% as a repellent for egg laying. <br/>-&nbsp;Application spray of NPV (nuclear polyhedrosis virus) @250 LE*/ha. (*LE = Larval Equivalent)<br/>-&nbsp;Various entomopathogenic fungi such as <i>Beauveria bassiana</i> and <i>Metarhizium anisopliae</i> @ 30 gram/10 liter have been effectively used to control lepidopteran larvae and other pests.<br/>-&nbsp;Release of the egg parasitoid, <i>Trichogramma chilonis</i> or <i>T. brasiliensis</i> @ 1 Lakh/ha coinciding with flower initiation at 15 days interval.<br/> -&nbsp;Spray fenvalerate 20 EC @ 10 ml/10 liter.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous)<br/>-&nbsp;Spray Profenofos 50EC @ 15ml/10 liter.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous)<br/>

Title: Yellow Mosaic in Black Gram
Authors:   Mr. R. Rajkumar (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Selection of improved black gram (<i>Vigna mungo</i>) resistant varieties such as VBN- 5 and 6 <br/>-&nbsp;Avoid sowing in acute summer months (April-May).<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid dense sowing and follow the recommended spacing of 30 x 10 cm.<br/>-&nbsp;Maintain proper field sanitation and free weed flora field.<br/>-&nbsp;Washing the plants especially under surface of leaves by using low volume sprayer.<br/>-&nbsp;Careful removal and destruction of infected plant portions during infection early stage.<br/>-&nbsp;Placing of yellow sticky traps at 20/acre or light traps at 1 per acre to attract and kill the vector whiteflies.<br/>-&nbsp;Sowing of barrier crops, such as sorghum, maize, or cumbu in bunds<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid application of nitrogenous fertilizers during growth phase.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Careful monitoring of the whitefly vector (<i>Bemisia tabaci</i>) incidence during the early phase of crop growth (up to 30 days).<br/>-&nbsp;Close observation of the leaf surface and adopt the plant protection measures when the Economic Threshold Level (ETL) reaches 5-10 adults or 20 nymphs/leaf.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spraying of 10% <i>Vitex negundo</i> (Nochi leaf) extract (or) 1% tobacco leaf extract. <br/>-&nbsp;Spraying of 5% neem seed kernel extract or 3% neem oil.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Severally infected plants turn yellow and sterile hence, no chemical is effective at this stage.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Treating the seeds with Imidacloprid 80% WS @ 2 grams/kg of seeds.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Neonicotinoids group. Systemic.<br/>-&nbsp;Acetamiprid 20% SP @ 0.5 grams/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Neonicotinoids group. Systemic with contact and stomach action.<br/>-&nbsp;Diafenthiuron 25% WP @ 2 grams/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class III. Thiourea group. Systemic.<br/>-&nbsp;Dimethoate 30% EC @ 2ml/litre of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous). Organophosphate group. Systemic.<br/>
Scientific Names: <b>Yellow Mosaic Virus</b>

Title: Cotton Mealy Bug
Authors:   Mr. Vijay Thokre, Mr. Nilesh Patkar, Dr. Malarvanan & Mr Rajkumar
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Destruction of cotton stalks.<br/>-&nbsp;Clean cultivation: Destroy alternate weed hosts growing on field bunds, water channels and wastelands in the area. <br/>-&nbsp;Summer ploughing to expose the life stages of the pest.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove alternate host plants (e.g. congress weed) and other weed hosts in and around fields.<br/>-&nbsp;Do not throw uprooted weeds in water channel.<br/>-&nbsp;Use the crop rotation practices (e.g. rice or corn based on the availability of water and awareness of husbandry practices) and burn the plant debris to clean field<br/>-&nbsp;It is important to remember that mealybug crawlers spread through human interventions such as spraying, irrigations, frequent movement through the infected area etc., so avoid these activities.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Observe for: Sooty mould on plant parts. Presence of ant colonies on the plants.<br/>-&nbsp;Take action when 5% of the plants are infested.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Biological Method: Encourage the activity of predators like <i>Cryptolaemus montrouzieri</i>, <i>Cheilomenes sexmaculata</i>.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Mealybugs at initial stage appear in small pockets, therefore, recommended insecticides are applied only in the infested spot, not in the entire crop.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Locate ant colonies and destroy them with drenching of chlorpyriphos 20 EC @ 2.5 ml/l of water or by application of 5% malathion dust @ 25 kg/ha. This operation can be carried out round the year.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Chlorpyrifos: WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous), organophosphate. Malathion: WHO Class III (Slightly hazardous), organophosphate.<br/>-&nbsp;Recommended doses of insecticides of carbamate (carbaryl 50 WP @ 2.5 kg/ha or thiodicarb 75 WP @ 625 gm/ha) and organophosphate (profenofos 50 EC @ 1,250 ml/ha, quinolphos 25 EC @ 2,000 ml/ha, acephate 75 SP @ 2,000 gm/ha or chlorpyriphos 25 EC @ 5l/ha) & add commercial stickers, spreaders or the khadhi soap (20gm / 10 lit of water) to degrade the waxy coating and spray rotation-wise in consecutive sprays.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Carbaryl: WHO Class II. Thiodicarb: WHO Class II , (Moderately Hazardous). Profenofos: WHO Class II , (Moderately Hazardous). Quinalophos: WHO Class II , (Moderately Hazardous). Acephate: WHO Class II , (Moderately Hazardous). Chlopyrifos: WHO Class II , (Moderately Hazardous), organophosphate<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Phenacoccus solenopsis</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_89652.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Destruction of cotton stalks.<br/>-&nbsp;Clean cultivation: Destroy alternate weed hosts growing on field bunds, water channels and wastelands in the area. <br/>-&nbsp;Summer ploughing to expose the life stages of the pest.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove alternate host plants (e.g. congress weed) and other weed hosts in and around fields.<br/>-&nbsp;Do not throw uprooted weeds in water channel.<br/>-&nbsp;Use the crop rotation practices (e.g. rice or corn based on the availability of water and awareness of husbandry practices) and burn the plant debris to clean field<br/>-&nbsp;It is important to remember that mealybug crawlers spread through human interventions such as spraying, irrigations, frequent movement through the infected area etc., so avoid these activities.<br/> -&nbsp;Observe for: Sooty mould on plant parts. Title: Early Shoot Borer on Sugarcane
Authors:   Dr. V.R.Saminathan (TNAU), Mr.G.Sudakar ( MSSRF), Dr. K.Chozhan (TNAU), Ms.D.S.Girija (MSSRF)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Planting to be done in early season (Dec  Jan) <br/>-&nbsp;Avoid moisture stress in the early stages of crop, e.g. by making small bundles of plant trash and put them in furrows.<br/>-&nbsp;Intercropping with dhaincha or pulses to repel the adults.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor for:  

<br/>-&nbsp;Presence of egg masses under the surface of the leaves. <br/>-&nbsp;Presence of dead hearts which can be easily pulled out.<br/>-&nbsp;Presence of bore holes at the collar region. <br/>-&nbsp;Light trap catches @ one per five hectare. <br/>-&nbsp;Monitoring through pheromone traps @ 5 per acre. Erect the trap at 15 days after planting. (Replace the septa once every three weeks.)<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray Granulosis Virus (750 numbers of diseased larvae) 200ml per acre.<br/>-&nbsp;Release <i>Trichogramma chilonis</i> 2cc per acre /release for three times at fifteen days interval starting from 30 days after planting.<br/>-&nbsp;Release 125 gravid females of <i>Sturmiopsis inferens</i>/ha on 30 and 45 days after planting.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray neem seed kernel extract 5%. (25 kg of NSK/ha dissolved in 500 litres of water).<br/>
    -&nbsp;Repeated use of same insecticide should be avoided.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid spraying insecticides up to five to seven days after parasitoid release.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray chlorantraniliprole 18.5% SC @ 375 ml per ha. Diamide pesticide. Stomach and contact poison.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class U (Unlikely to present acute hazard in normal use).<br/>-&nbsp;Spray fipronil 5% SC @ 2 litres per ha. Phenylpyrazole. Contact and stomach poison.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II (Moderately hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Spray quinalphos 25% EC 2 litres per ha. Organophosphate. Contact and stomach poison.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class II.<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Chilo infuscatellus</i>
