Title:  Bhendi Powdery mildew
Authors:   Mr. Nilesh Ganesh Patkar, Dr. Raja Ramesh (Assistant Professor, ICAR-KVK, TNAU)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Avoid  planting during November to February  in endemic areas<br/>-&nbsp;Maintain proper plant spacing (45 x 30 cm)<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid excessive application of nitrogenous fertilizer<br/>
    -&nbsp;Observe for white powdery growth on upper surface <br/>-&nbsp;Observe for  shedding of severely infected   leaves <br/>-&nbsp;Apply direct control measures when disease first observed<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray <i>Ampelomyces quisqualis</i> @ 2% conc. at disease initiation stage<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use of personal protective equipment (PPEs) such as gloves, mask, goggles and keeping antidotes are mandatory by the spray applicators<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray Tridemorph @ 0.05% or Wettable sulphur @ 0.3% or Propineb @ 0.25% at disease initiation stage and repeat at weekly intervals. .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Tridemorph falls in WHO class II. Wettable sulphur falls in WHO class III. Propineb falls in WHO class U<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Erysiphe cichoracearum</i>

Title: Pomegranate Bacterial blight
Authors:   Mr. Nilesh Ganesh Patkar, Dr. Raja Ramesh, Mr. Ganeshmoorthy, R.
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Plant orchard with disease free planting material. <br/>-&nbsp;Remove fallen leaves and debris from the orchard and burn.<br/>-&nbsp;Proper spacing of 5x5m to avoid the disease and pest incidence.<br/>-&nbsp;Pruning is done after harvest is completed during December. <br/>
    -&nbsp;Observe dark brown to brown black necrotic spots, surrounded by a yellow hallow or water soaked margin on leaves and fruit. <br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor crop regularly and remove affected plant debris from the field <br/>-&nbsp;Spraying of garlic extract 5% at 7 day intervals  after initiation of the new leaves has been found to be effective<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use of personal protective equipment (PPEs) such as gloves, mask, goggles and keeping antidotes are mandatory by the spray applicators.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray Bordeaux mixture (0.5% except 1% just after pruning, rest period) .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Copper oxychloride and copper hydroxide. WHO classification Class II Moderately Hazardous<br/>-&nbsp;Alternate with spray of streptocycline (5g/10 litre) or 2-bromo, 2-nitro propane-1, 3-diol (5g/10 l) mixed with copper oxychloride or copper hydroxide (20-25g/ 10 litre) and spreader sticker (0.5ml/litre) at 10-15 days interval depending on weather conditions. The spray interval of 7 days should be adopted in <i>mrig bahar</i> and 10-14 days in <i>hasta</i> and <i>ambe</i> bahar season.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Bronopol: WHO classification II (moderately hazardous).<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Xanthomonas axonopodis pv. punicae</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_99464.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_99466.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Plant orchard with disease free planting material. <br/>-&nbsp;Remove fallen leaves and debris from the orchard and burn.<br/>-&nbsp;Proper spacing of 5x5m to avoid the disease and pest incidence.<br/>-&nbsp;Pruning is done after harvest is completed during December. <br/> -&nbsp;Observe dark brown to brown black necrotic spots, surrounded by a yellow hallow or water soaked margin on leaves and fruit. <br/> -&nbsp;Monitor crop regularly and remove affected plant debris from the field <br/>-&nbsp;Spraying of garlic extract 5% at 7 day intervals  after initiation of the new leaves has been found to be effective<br/> -&nbsp;Use of personal protective equipment (PPEs) such as gloves, mask, goggles and keeping antidotes are mandatory by the spray applicators.<br/> -&nbsp;Spray Bordeaux mixture (0.5% except 1% just after pruning, rest period) .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Copper oxychloride and copper hydroxide. Title: Coffee Berry Borer
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;The coffee berry borer causes most damage in established and large scale coffee plantations where cultural practices are scarce or non-existent. The following cultural practices are important to reduce the damages:<br/>-&nbsp;Use the same variety<br/>-&nbsp;Highly shaded coffee plantations favour the proliferation of the borer. Prune and thin shading trees and coffee trees to avoid excessive shade conditions<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid movement of infested fruits in areas free of the coffee berry borer<br/>-&nbsp;Place mulch such as straw on the soil<br/>-&nbsp;Balanced fertilization<br/>-&nbsp;Control weeds<br/>-&nbsp;Timely harvest (as soon as possible)<br/>-&nbsp;After harvest, rigorously collect by hand all of the remaining fruits attached to the branches (especially the lower branches) or on the soil. Bury the fruits at 50 cm or boil if infestation level is high<br/>-&nbsp;Elimination of decaying or abandoned coffee plantations<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor developing fruits each week for infestation, from the eighth week after flowering. Divide the cropland into plots of 5 ha. From 20 plants/plot, collect 100 random fruits/plant. Cut the fruits in 2 parts and check for eggs, larvae, nymphs and adults. Sample and monitor the fallen fruits too<br/>-&nbsp;To monitor adult population levels, set 20 baited traps/ha for 3 months at the onset of the rains. See the description of the technique in the direct control section<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are black beetles. The female is 1.4-1.6 mm long X 1 mm wide, and the male is much smaller. Larvae are white, with a black head and legless<br/>-&nbsp;The female perforates around the apex and the perforations deepen towards the centre of the seeds forming galleries. If the perforations are crusts, brown or rotten, it means that the larvae are inside the seeds and protected<br/>-&nbsp;Take control measures when infestation level reaches 5 %<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use baited traps (16/hectare) to catch adults flying after the first rains or in inter-harvest period (when the coffee berry borer is emerging and a few fruits are available). Fill in half the handmade traps (plastic bottles with two openings) with bait (1:1 ethanol: methanol) and place in the middle of the trees (1.5 m). Check every 5 days<br/>-&nbsp;Fumigation in the morning with the fungus <i>Beauveria bassiana</i> 1 kg/ha when the coffee berry borer begins to attack the fruit (8 weeks after flowering)<br/>-&nbsp;Collect ripe and dry fruits every two weeks to remove reproductive sites of the coffee berry borer<br/>-&nbsp;Release of parasitoids if available in your country such as <i>Cephalonomia stephanoderis</i> or <i>Prorops nasuta</i><br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Hypothenemus hampei</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_106692.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_106693.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_106694.img?width=800&height=640
Content:

If the perforations are crusts, brown or rotten, it means that the larvae are inside the seeds and protected<br/>-&nbsp;Take control measures when infestation level reaches 5 %<br/> -&nbsp;Use baited traps (16/hectare) to catch adults flying after the first rains or in inter-harvest period (when the coffee berry borer is emerging and a few fruits are available). Fill in half the handmade traps (plastic bottles with two openings) with bait (1:1 ethanol: methanol) and place in the middle of the trees (1.5 m). Check every 5 days<br/>-&nbsp;Fumigation in the morning with the fungus <i>Beauveria bassiana</i> 1 kg/ha when the coffee berry borer begins to attack the fruit (8 weeks after flowering)<br/>-&nbsp;Collect ripe and dry fruits every two weeks to remove reproductive sites of the coffee berry borer<br/>-&nbsp;Release of parasitoids if available in your country such as <i>Cephalonomia stephanoderis</i> or <i>Prorops nasuta</i><br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Citrus red mite
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Damage is more severe when the plants are suffering from water stress. Therefore maintain a good irrigation system<br/>-&nbsp;Favour the presence of plants that host predators such as the castor bean (<i>Ricinus communis</i>)<br/>-&nbsp;Control ants that protect mites<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the repetitive use of broad-spectrum acaricides as they kill natural enemies (predatory mites and thrips, beetles and lacewings) and create resistance from the pest<br/>-&nbsp;Prune to maintain good air circulation within the tree<br/>-&nbsp;Control weeds<br/>
    -&nbsp;Every two weeks, sample 10 leaves taken randomly from 30 trees for the presence of eggs, mobile nymphs, and natural enemies<br/>-&nbsp;Monitor the leaves, fruits and young branches<br/>-&nbsp;Increase the frequency of sampling in warmer and dryer areas<br/>-&nbsp;Eggs are spherical and bright red<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are globular and dark red and have white hairs on their backs<br/>-&nbsp;The nymphs and adults feed on the upper surface of the leaf and on the fruits which produces discoloration and a silver dots<br/>-&nbsp;Severe attacks can result in reduced vigor, dieback and premature falling of leaves<br/>
    -&nbsp;Release of predatory mites if commercially available in your country (<i>Euseius stipulates</i>, <i>E. tularensis</i>, <i>Amblyseius</i> spp.)<br/>-&nbsp;Release the predatory mites if the pest is not naturally controlled and the natural balance is broken by the repetitive applications of broad-spectrum acaricides<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Panonychus citri</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_106863.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_106864.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Damage is more severe when the plants are suffering from water stress. Therefore maintain a good irrigation system<br/>-&nbsp;Favour the presence of plants that host predators such as the castor bean (<i>Ricinus communis</i>)<br/>-&nbsp;Control ants that protect mites<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the repetitive use of broad-spectrum acaricides as they kill natural enemies (predatory mites and thrips, beetles and lacewings) and create resistance from the pest<br/>-&nbsp;Prune to maintain good air circulation within the tree<br/>-&nbsp;Control weeds<br/> -&nbsp;Every two weeks, sample 10 leaves taken randomly from 30 trees for the presence of eggs, mobile nymphs, and natural enemies<br/>-&nbsp;Monitor the leaves, fruits and young branches<br/>-&nbsp;Increase the frequency of sampling in warmer and dryer areas<br/>-&nbsp;Eggs are spherical and bright red<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are globular and dark red and have white hairs on their backs<br/>-&nbsp;The nymphs and adults feed on the upper surface of the leaf and on the fruits which produces discoloration and a silver dots<br/>-&nbsp;Severe attacks can result in reduced vigor, dieback and premature falling of leaves<br/> -&nbsp;Release of predatory mites if commercially available in your country (<i>Euseius stipulates</i>, <i>E. tularensis</i>, <i>Amblyseius</i> spp.)<br/>-&nbsp;Release the predatory mites if the pest is not naturally controlled and the natural balance is broken by the repetitive applications of broad-spectrum acaricides<br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Title: Erect prickly pear in pasture
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Prevent pasture from degrading by moving livestock regularly and sewing seed. Degraded pasture can lead to infestations by the weed<br/>
    -&nbsp;Succulent erect, spreading shrub, 0.5-1.3 (-2) m high. Forms thickets.<br/>-&nbsp;Cladodes (modified stems): blue-green, longer than broad (10-20 cm long)<br/>-&nbsp;Spines: 1-2 per cladode, 1.5-4 cm long, flattened and yellow.<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: Cylindrical, minute and shed early<br/>-&nbsp;Flowers: Yellow and large (56 cm long and 56 cm wide)<br/>-&nbsp;Fruits: Berries, green turning red-purple as they mature, egg-shaped (46 cm long and 2.53 cm wide), outer surface smooth with clusters of barbed hairs or bristles, narrowed at the base, purple sour pulp, white seeds.<br/>-&nbsp;Look out for this weed by in disturbed areas, savannah, grassland, and on riverbanks in arid to semi-arid regions<br/>
    -&nbsp;Manually remove the weed if infestations are low. <b>Wear protective gloves and clothing to protect skin from the spines</b>. Remove the weed before it flowers to prevent spread<br/>-&nbsp;Burn uprooted material if there is enough dry material to burn and if it is permitted in your area. This will prevent the material taking root<br/>-&nbsp;Burning can help control small, isolated stands if the fire is hot but will not penetrate large stands<br/>-&nbsp;Mechanical control of the weed is not recommended unless infestations are low since small broken fragments are able to root in soil, sometimes making the infestation worse<br/>-&nbsp;Common biological controlm agents include a Phycitid moth, <i>Cactoblastis cactorum</i> and a cochineal insect, <i>Dactylopius opuntiae</i>. See if these are available in your country<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Opuntia stricta</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108041.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108042.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108043.img?width=800&height=640

Title: Diamondback moth (DBM) on cabbage
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Rotate cabbage, rape, broccoli or cauliflower with any of the following crops: tomato, onion, maize, or sweet potato<br/>-&nbsp;Plant early in the season to avoid the period of diamond back moth build-up <br/>-&nbsp;Remove and bury/burn crop debris including weeds at least 4 weeks prior to sowing to reduce DBM population<br/>-&nbsp;Plant seedling beds away from production fields<br/>-&nbsp;Transplant only healthy seedlings free of eggs, caterpillars and pupae of the DBM and other pests<br/>-&nbsp;Intercrop with tomato, chilies, marigolds or mustard. Mustard acts as a trap crop for adult moths. Plant two rows of mustard for every 25 of cabbage<br/>-&nbsp;Slash left-over cabbage stems after harvest to avoid regrowth which can act as a diamond back moth breeding area<br/>-&nbsp;After harvest, plough the field to expose any pests harbouring in the soil<br/>
    -&nbsp;Additional relevant crops: Crucifer family (rape, broccoli, cauliflower, chinese cabbage)<br/>-&nbsp;Inspect crop weekly, especially the underside of leaves<br/>-&nbsp;Look for small, grey moths, which have a diamond pattern on their wings when at rest<br/>-&nbsp;Look for caterpillars which are yellowish green to green and around 1 cm long when fully grown. They hang from a web when disturbed and tend to appear at the four leaf stage of cabbage growth<br/>-&nbsp;Look for damage on leaves: irregular holes, windowing and skeletonised underside<br/>-&nbsp;Detect DBM adults using pheromone traps, placing 2-4 traps/Ha. Check traps twice a week early in the morning. Use control when 5 + adult moths are caught in a trap<br/>-&nbsp;Alternatively, you may consider direct control when you notice 2 to 3 young larvae per plant<br/>
    -&nbsp;Uproot and bury or burn cabbage debris, if burning is permitted in your country<br/>-&nbsp;Crush larvae by hand if noticed when numbers are few<br/>-&nbsp;Apply aqueous solution of botanical pesticide neem or tephrosia (40g/L soak over night and seive)<br/>-&nbsp;Alternatively spray chilli mixed with water and few chopped pieces of bar soap or drops of liquid soap<br/>-&nbsp;Plant trap crops such as mustard and rape at the edges of the the field to reduce DBM attack<br/>-&nbsp;Parasitic wasps such as <i>Diadegma</i> and <i>Cotesia vestalis</i> spp. can reduce DBM populations. See if these biological controls are available in your country<br/>-&nbsp;Use overhead irrigation which dislodges the pest from the plant surface<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Plutella xylostella</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108139.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108140.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108141.img?width=800&height=640

Title: Water hyacinth in rice
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Minimise the level of nutrients in water by planting trees to stablise ground near rice fields and limiting fertilizer use<br/>
    -&nbsp;Free-floating, aquatic plant that may be anchored in shallow water. Often forms thick mats<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: dark green, shiny, hairless, oval to egg-shaped to almost rounded (2-25 cm long and 2-15 cm wide), thick and shiny; swollen bladder-like stems (30 cm long)<br/>-&nbsp;Flowers: pale violet or blue (4-6 cm long and 3.5-5 cm wide); clusters of 8-10<br/>-&nbsp;Fruit: capsules (a dry fruit that opens at maturity) (10-15 mm long), containing very fine seeds<br/>-&nbsp;Roots: long and feathery <br/>-&nbsp;Runners: 10 cm long, produced across the water surface to give rise to new plants<br/>-&nbsp;Look for this weed in irrigation channels, dams, ponds, floodplains, swamps, wetlands and slow-moving rivers<br/>
    -&nbsp;Small infestations can be manually removed, including adults and eggs<br/>-&nbsp;Do not mechanically chop or shred the weed - this creates fragments which can form new plants<br/>-&nbsp;Biological control agents include: the mite <i>Orthogalumna terebrantis</i>, the moth <i>Acigona infusella</i>, the miridae <i>Eccritotarsus catarinensis</i> and the weevils <i>Neochetina eichhorniae</i> and <i>Neochetina bruchi</i>. See if they are available in your country <br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Eichhornia crassipes</i>

Title: Golden apple snail on rice
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Prevent snails from entering field by filtering the water entering the field through net traps (5 mm mesh)<br/>-&nbsp;Clean equipment (especially harvester) before moving into another field to prevent spreading the pest<br/>-&nbsp;Before planting, make drains in the rice field and add water to the drain. Allow snails to migrate to the water and collect snails and destroy<br/>-&nbsp;Plant by transplanting rice instead of direct seedling. Transplant seedlings at 25-30 days<br/>-&nbsp;Rotate rice with dry land crop and/or allow for fallow periods between flood rice<br/>-&nbsp;Release natural predators. i.e. ducks (before or after planting)<br/>
    -&nbsp;Scout 3 times/week<br/>-&nbsp;Look for bright or pale pink coloured egg masses which are laid above water on vegetation and other firm substrates (e.g. rocks)<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are yellow-brown to greenish-brown or dark chesnut, sometimes with dark brown spiral bands of variable number and thickness. 35-60 mm tall<br/>-&nbsp;If pest population density exceeds 2 snails per square meter, use Direct Control<br/>-&nbsp;Bamboo stake trap every 5 m to observe egg mass<br/>-&nbsp;Observe natural enemies, e.g.  penbilled stork (or Egret). Small water bug.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Put sticks (approximately 30 cm long) in rice field. Allow snail to lay eggs and then collect and destroy eggs from the stick<br/>-&nbsp;Make shallow trenches before draining water from the field. Snails will congregate in the trenches and can be collected with a basket/net<br/>-&nbsp;Remove all vegetation and obstacles around fields to reduce the areas where snails can lay there eggs. Snails are forced to lay their eggs on bare ground where they are more vulnerable<br/>-&nbsp;Have field day to collect and destroy snails<br/>-&nbsp;Release ducks (natural enemies) into the field at the tillering stage (5-10 ducks/ha for 1 month)<br/>-&nbsp;If low population, catch by hand and destroy or make snail compost or feed to ducks<br/>-&nbsp;Stock predatory fish such as <i>Cyprinus carpio</i> and <i>Oreochromis niloticus</i><br/>-&nbsp;Trap: Take bundles of Papaya, Sweet Potato or Tapioca (Cassava) leaves. Place them in water along edge of field for at least 24 hours. Then collect and destroy snails<br/>-&nbsp;Use net screen traps on water supply inlets<br/>-&nbsp;Remove snail and egg mass from field (Mechanical method). Use bamboo stake trap (egg mass). Hand picking (egg mass - adult). i.e. plant leaf trap. papaya leaf). rice brand with nylon net  rap (once a week). Thais local wisdom trap (water bottle traps)<br/>-&nbsp;If snail density is high then use multiple control methods<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Lantana in pasture
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Use clean pasture seed to reduce the chance of contamination<br/>-&nbsp;Remove all existing hedges by cutting and digging out the roots (15cm depth)

<br/>-&nbsp;Move livestock out of previously infested fields. Sow land with pasture and/or native vegetation. Growth of new vegetation can outcompete the weed.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Perennial woody shrub, 2-5m high, erect or scrambling. <br/>-&nbsp;Branches with small thorns<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: yellow-green to green, rough, 2-12 cm in length<br/>-&nbsp;Flowers: multicolored (yellow, orange, white, pale violet, pink or red), in dense clusters ca. 4 cm across.<br/>-&nbsp;Fruits: round, fleshy, 2-seeded, each 5 mm wide, green turning purple then blue-black. Arranged in dense, round clusters<br/>-&nbsp;Look out for this weed on disturbed land, plantations, forest edges/gaps, gassland, water courses, lowlands and gullies<br/>
    -&nbsp;<b>Do not control with grazing as the weed is poisonous to livestock<b><br/>-&nbsp;Removal of small infestations may be possible by cutting and digging out the roots (15 cm depth)<br/>-&nbsp;Remove flower heads prior to seed set to prevent further spread<br/>-&nbsp;Slashing and burning can stimulate regrowth of the weed. Combine with other control methods<br/>-&nbsp;Control will need to be repeated, potentially over many years as the weed is difficult to get rid of<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Lantana camara</i>

Title: American serpentine leafminer on vegetables
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Use tolerant crop varieties if available<br/>-&nbsp;Where possible, cover small plants with transparent plastic (such as polytunnels) to prevent insects from laying eggs<br/>-&nbsp;Weed out leguminous plants which can be alternative hosts such as sweet clover, <i>Lathyrus</i>, and many others<br/>-&nbsp;Destory and bury the remains of broadleaf weeds as they can harbour leaf miners<br/>-&nbsp;Deep plough crop residues<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor weekly at all growth stages of crop<br/>-&nbsp;Look for tiny thread-like tunnels (mines) on the upper leaf sides<br/>-&nbsp;Look for larvae inside tunnels or on the upper side of leaves (tiny legless maggots with no separate head, whitish transparent and later yellow-orange) <br/>-&nbsp;Threshold: At 8 to 12 infected plants per 100 plants, consider green direct control action  <br/>-&nbsp;Use glue traps or yellow sticky traps to see whether leafminers are present<br/>
    -&nbsp;Hand pick heavily infested leaves with many leaf miner tunnels, and destroy them by crushing or burying them, or feeding them to cattle if palatable<br/>-&nbsp;Spray neem extract against larvae onto leaves in the early morning or late evening. Add a sticker such as liquid soap to the neem water mix. Reach good leaf coverage. Neem slightly enters the leaves and reaches some of the larvae inside the tunnel<br/>-&nbsp;If possible cover soil under infested plants with plastic sheets to prevent larvae from reaching the ground and pupating<br/>-&nbsp;Use glue traps or yellow sticky traps to trap leafminers<br/>-&nbsp;Biological controls of leafminers include parasitoids (e.g.<i>Chrysonotomyia punctiventris</i> and <i>Ganaspidium hunteri</i>), nematodes (e.g. <i>Steinernema carpocapsae</i>, bacteria and fungi<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Liriomyza trifolii</i>

Content:
-&nbsp;Use tolerant crop varieties if available<br/>-&nbsp;Where possible, cover small plants with transparent plastic (such as polytunnels) to prevent insects from laying eggs<br/>-&nbsp;Weed out leguminous plants which can be alternative hosts such as sweet clover, <i>Lathyrus</i>, and many others<br/>-&nbsp;Destory and bury the remains of broadleaf weeds as they can harbour leaf miners<br/>-&nbsp;Deep plough crop residues<br/> -&nbsp;Monitor weekly at all growth stages of crop<br/>-&nbsp;Look for tiny thread-like tunnels (mines) on the upper leaf sides<br/>-&nbsp;Look for larvae inside tunnels or on the upper side of leaves (tiny legless maggots with no separate head, whitish transparent and later yellow-orange) <br/>-&nbsp;Threshold: At 8 to 12 infected plants per 100 plants, consider green direct control action  <br/>-&nbsp;Use glue traps or yellow sticky traps to see whether leafminers are present<br/> -&nbsp;Hand pick heavily infested leaves with many leaf miner tunnels, and destroy them by crushing or burying them, or feeding them to cattle if palatable<br/>-&nbsp;Spray neem extract against larvae onto leaves in the early morning or late evening. Title: Ironwood in pasture
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Use clean pasture seed to reduce the chance of contamination<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid movement of livestock though infested areas to prevent spread of seed<br/>-&nbsp;Prevent water runoff from infested land to non-infested land<br/>-&nbsp;Maintain a regular weeding regime before the plants grow big in order to facilitate uprooting<br/>-&nbsp;Remove Prosopis from roadsides, wastelands, field boundaries and other areas that serve as source of infestation<br/>
    -&nbsp;Perennial, woody shrub 3-5m or tree growing up to 15 m height<br/>-&nbsp;Bark: ough grey-green, becomes scaly with age<br/>-&nbsp;Branches: often multi-stemmed, especially following cutbacks, with abundant large and very sharp thorns (up to 5 cm)<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: doubly-divided composed (6-8cm long), 12-25 pairs of oblong leaflets per leaf<br/>-&nbsp;Flowers: fragrant golden-yellow, dense spikes about 5-10 cm long<br/>-&nbsp;Fruit: cylindrical or slightly irregularly curved green pods which turns yellow upon ripening (10-20 cm long) containing 10-20 hard oval or elliptic, brown seeds<br/>-&nbsp;Regularly check edges of waterbodies, grazing areas and animal gathering areas and consider direct control if present<br/>-&nbsp;Check for growth of trees where there are animal dropping/dungs since seed passes through animal dungs and germinate<br/>-&nbsp;Consider direct control as soon as the weed observed in the field<br/>
    -&nbsp;Seedlings up to 20 cm in height can be hand pulled<br/>-&nbsp;Cut the weed before seed production to prevent spread<br/>-&nbsp;Uproot trees or cut below ground to remove crown in order to prevent coppices<br/>-&nbsp;Follow up weeding consistently to achieve longer term control<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Prosopis juliflora</i>

Title: Kariba weed in rice
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Minimise the level of nutrients in water by limiting fertilizer use<br/>-&nbsp;Use of containment fences across the waterways to prevent introduction of weeds to paddy fields<br/>-&nbsp;Use water from tanks free form Salvinia<br/>
    -&nbsp;Evergreen, mat-forming, free-floating fern, branching horizontal stems (up to 625 cm long and 1.2 cm thick), submerged feathery roots<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: Green or yellowish-green fronds, in pairs, oval (26 cm long and 1015 mm wide); almost impossible to wet due to a covering of fine egg-beater-shaped hairs (13 mm long) on upper surface; undersides covered in matted brown hairs.<br/>-&nbsp;Flowers: none<br/>-&nbsp;Fruits: None, reproduces from detached fragments<br/>-&nbsp;Look out for weed in drainage ditches, irrigation channels, dams, ponds, swamps, wetlands, lakes and slow-moving rivers<br/>
    -&nbsp;Manually remove weed if infestation is small<br/>-&nbsp;Biological control options include the salvina weevil, <i>Cyrtobagus salviniae</i>. See if they are available in your country

<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Serpentine leaf miner on beans
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Use tolerant crop varieties if available<br/>-&nbsp;Where possible, cover small plants with transparent plastic (such as polytunnels) to prevent insects from laying eggs<br/>-&nbsp;Weed out leguminous plants which can be alternative hosts such as sweet clover, Lathyrus, and many others. <br/>-&nbsp;Plant maize around beans because maize acts as a barrier against the leaf miners<br/>-&nbsp;Rotate beans with cereals, and not with legumes, tomatoes, garlic, onions, groundnut, rape, melon or cucumber.<br/>-&nbsp;Tomato is a host plant for <i>L. trifolii</i> <br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor weekly at all growth stages of crop<br/>-&nbsp;Look for tiny thread-like tunnels (mines) on the upper leaf sides<br/>-&nbsp;Look for larvae inside tunnels or on the upper side of leaves (tiny legless maggots with no separate head, whitish, transparent and later yellow-orange) <br/>-&nbsp;Threshold: At 8 to 12 infected plants (with 5-6 infected leaves) per 100 plants, consider green direct control action<br/>-&nbsp;If, after green direct control, leaf miner damage still increases up to 20 to 30 infected plants per 100 plants, then consider chemical action<br/>-&nbsp;Use yellow sticky traps to assess the presence of <i>L. trifolii</i><br/>
    -&nbsp;Hand pick heavily infested leaves with many leaf miner tunnels, and destroy them by crushing or burying them, or feeding them to cattle<br/>-&nbsp;Spray neem extract against larvae onto leaves in the early morning or late evening. Add a sticker such as liquid soap to the neem water mix. Reach good leaf coverage. Neem slightly enters the leaves and reaches some of the larvae inside the tunnel<br/>-&nbsp;If possible, cover soil under infested plants with plastic sheets to prevent larvae from reaching the ground and pupating<br/>-&nbsp;Use yellow sticky traps to reduce numbers of <i>L. trifolii</i><br/>-&nbsp;Parasitoids, and to a lesser extent to nematodes, bacteria and fungi, are used for biological control of leafminers<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Liriomyza trifolii</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108159.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108160.img?width=800&height=640

Title: Siam weed
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Purchase certified crop seed and animal feed to reduce the chance of contamination<br/>-&nbsp;As seeds can travel on animals and machinery, do not allow animals into an affected area, limit machinery use in the area and keep machinery clean to prevent spread<br/>-&nbsp;Keep crop healthy to maximize competitiveness - use optimal water and fertilizer levels<br/>-&nbsp;Plant competitive species such as <i>Leucaena leucocephala</i>, <i>Tephrosia purpurea</i> and <i>Brachiaria decumbens</i> to surpress the weed<br/>-&nbsp;Plant cover crops such as <i>Pueraria phaseoloides</i>, <i>Calopogonium mucunoides</i>, <i>Centrosema pubescens</i> or <i>Vigna unguiculata</i> to prevent weed establishment and spread<br/>
    -&nbsp;Herbaceous to woody perennial herb; forms dense bushes on the ground about 2 m high; climber; becomes woody when mature<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: Soft, green, hairy, roughly triangular; margins have 1-5 teeth when mature (younger leaves have none); numerous small dots on underside; 5-14 cm long, 2.5-8 cm broad<br/>-&nbsp;Roots: Fibrous; shallow  do not penetrate beyond 20-30 cm in most soils<br/>-&nbsp;Flowerheads: White or pale blue-ish-lilac; appear fluffy; many<br/>-&nbsp;Leaf shape, flower colour and structure are similar to that of <i>Mikania</i> species. <br/>-&nbsp;Look along waterways and transport routes. Seeds disperse via water, wind animals and vehicles<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use a combination of methods to control the weed. Carry these out before the weed flowers or sets seed to prevent spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Hand-pull seedlings and young plants. Repeat every 2-3 months afterwards due to rapid regrowth<br/>-&nbsp;Completely dig out root system by hand or by using machinery  this will be easier if soil is wet. Repeat twice during the growing season.<br/>-&nbsp;Cutting is only a short-term solution as weed will grow back from the roots<br/>-&nbsp;Burn weeds that have been pulled up or cut (if permitted in your country). If not destroyed, they can take root and grow<br/>-&nbsp;Biological controls that have shown effectiveness include, <i>Pareuchaetes pseudoinsulata</i>, <i>Cecidochares connexa</i>, <i>Actinote thalia pyrrha</i>, <i>Actinote anteas</i>, <i>Acalitus adoratus</i>, <i>Calycomyza eupatorivora</i>, <i>Pareuchaetes insulata</i> and <i>Lixus aemulus</i>. See if these are available in your country.<br/>-&nbsp;It is difficult to kill this weed with chemicals because of its many tubers and roots from which it can regenerate, and its waxy leaves<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Chromolaena odorata</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108173.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108174.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108175.img?width=800&height=640

Title: East African couchgrass
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Purchase certified crop seed and animal feed to reduce the chance of contamination<br/>-&nbsp;As seeds can travel on animals and machinery, do not allow animals into an affected area, limit machinery use in the area and keep machinery clean to prevent spread<br/>-&nbsp;Keep crop healthy to maximize competitiveness - use optimal water and fertilizer levels <br/>
    -&nbsp;Creeping, perennial grass, rhizomatous; 50-100 cm high<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: flat; bluish-green 15 cm long, 2-8 mm wide<br/>-&nbsp;Inflorescence: 2-25 racemes. Spikelets in pairs<br/>-&nbsp;Rhizomes: can be over 1 m deep in soil forming dense mats<br/>-&nbsp;Fruits: elliptical in various shades of light brown, grey and purple<br/>-&nbsp;Looks similar to Cynodon dactylon, but <i>C. dactylon</i> has a membraneous ligule where the leaf blade joins the sheath<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use a forked hoe to remove rhizomes. Ensure removal is thorough to prevent resprouting<br/>-&nbsp;Till land to expose and dessicate rhizomes, but be aware that this action may also spread fragments which can grow new plants<br/>-&nbsp;For coffee, plant <i>Guizotia abyssinica</i> and <i>Linum usitatissimum</i> as cover crops to suppress weed <br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Torpedo grass
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Clean tillage equipment and other farm machinery to prevent weed rhizomes being spread<br/>-&nbsp;Do not accept potentially contaminated materials, e.g. fill dirt, hay, and clean vegetation off boat trailers and machinery<br/>-&nbsp;Limit the presence of open and disturbed areas by preventing overgrazing<br/>-&nbsp;</b>Use legumes as cover crops to smother weed in plantation crops in sufficiently humid climatic conditions<b><br/>-&nbsp;Control established populations near waterways to prevent weed spreading to fields<br/>-&nbsp;For pasture, encourage a healthy and diverse ecosystem to deter infestation<br/>
    -&nbsp;Perennial grass, clusters of flowers up to 50-100 cm high<br/>-&nbsp;Look out for it in wetlands and along waterways. Water and storm events can spread it<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: bright green; stiff; sparsely hairy on upper surface; edges of leaf coverings have long white hairs; 15-20 cm long, 1 cm wide<br/>-&nbsp;Rhizomes: grow horizontally; up to 20 cm deep; several metres long <br/>-&nbsp;Flowers: spikelets (flower clusters) 2-flowered; pale green, sometimes tinged with purple; 2.5-3 mm long<br/>-&nbsp;Similar to <i>Panicum repentellum</i>, distinguished by its nerveless lower glume (a small modified leaf surrounding the spikelet)<br/>-&nbsp;Rhizomes can be spread via soil transport<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use a combination of continuous tillage, hand weeding, cutting and burning. Using only one of these methods can result in rapid regrowth from rhizomes.<br/>-&nbsp;Tillage must be deep in order to disturb as many of the rhizomes as possible. Do this under dry conditions so the exposed rhizomes dessicate<br/>-&nbsp;</b>Use legumes as cover crops to smother weed in plantation crops in sufficiently humid climatic conditions<b><br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Sida
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Purchase and sow certified crop and pasture seed to reduce the chance of contamination<br/>-&nbsp;Seeds are spread by animals - do not allow livestock into an affected area to avoid spread<br/>-&nbsp;Seeds travel on machinery - clean equipment and vehicules after work in infested areas<br/>-&nbsp;Prevent spread of Sida by slashing or mowing before it flowers<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid overgrazing to prevent establishment of the weed in disturbed patches<br/>
    -&nbsp;Small shrub (0.5-1.5m) with lanceolate leaves with dented egde. <br/>-&nbsp;The flowers are yellow, solitary, 1-2 cm in diameter and on a short stalk 0.3-0.8 cm long. Each flower has six petals and produces seeds with two awns, thus attaching easily to animal fur<br/>-&nbsp;The fruit is a hard, brown capsule, 3-5 mm in diameter, breaking into 5-8 triangular segments<br/>-&nbsp;Regularly check waste and other ruderal sites, because these are usually the first invaded by Sida<br/>-&nbsp;Check for flowering and seed development to prevent seed set, spread and build up of seed bank<br/>
    -&nbsp;Dig up single plants prior to onset of flowering, ensuring the taproot is severed well below the crown to prevent regrowth<br/>-&nbsp;Plow or hand-weed after the first rains have induced germination, followed by sowing of dense pasture or cover crop<br/>-&nbsp;Larger areas can be controlled by repeated cultivation until the soil seed bank is depleted<br/>-&nbsp;Competitive grasses may be effective in suppressing Sida, when combined with other control measures<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Sida acuta</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108211.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108212.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108213.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Purchase and sow certified crop and pasture seed to reduce the chance of contamination<br/>-&nbsp;Seeds are spread by animals - do not allow livestock into an affected area to avoid spread<br/>-&nbsp;Seeds travel on machinery - clean equipment and vehicules after work in infested areas<br/>-&nbsp;Prevent spread of Sida by slashing or mowing before it flowers<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid overgrazing to prevent establishment of the weed in disturbed patches<br/> -&nbsp;Small shrub (0.5-1.5m) with lanceolate leaves with dented egde. <br/>-&nbsp;The flowers are yellow, solitary, 1-2 cm in diameter and on a short stalk 0.3-0.8 cm long. Each flower has six petals and produces seeds with two awns, thus attaching easily to animal fur<br/>-&nbsp;The fruit is a hard, brown capsule, 3-5 mm in diameter, breaking into 5-8 triangular segments<br/>-&nbsp;Regularly check waste and other ruderal sites, because these are usually the first invaded by Sida<br/>-&nbsp;Check for flowering and seed development to prevent seed set, spread and build up of seed bank<br/> -&nbsp;Dig up single plants prior to onset of flowering, ensuring the taproot is severed well below the crown to prevent regrowth<br/>-&nbsp;Plow or hand-weed after the first rains have induced germination, followed by sowing of dense pasture or cover crop<br/>-&nbsp;Larger areas can be controlled by repeated cultivation until the soil seed bank is depleted<br/>-&nbsp;Competitive grasses may be effective in suppressing Sida, when combined with other control measures<br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Mexican sunflower
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Clean material, boots, clothing and vehicles when moving out of infested areas<br/>-&nbsp;Seeds are spread by animals - do not allow livestock into an affected area to avoid spread<br/>-&nbsp;Species is spread by water - use only water from sources free of the weed and avoid runoff from surrounding infested fields<br/>-&nbsp;The species is spread by wind - limit seed set in surrounding populations<br/>-&nbsp;Mulching using plastic sheeting or wood shavings reduces establishment from seed<br/>
    -&nbsp;Annual or perennial herbaceous broadleaved shrub, woody at the base [23 (5) m high]<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves greyish-green, finely hairy on underside giving a grey appearance. Simple, 35 (7) pointed lobes (633 cm long and 522cm wide)<br/>-&nbsp;Flowers bright yellow, daisy or sunflower-like (up to 10 cm across), carried on long and swollen stalks (730 cm long)<br/>-&nbsp;Regularly check fields for presence of the weed and consider direct control when present<br/>-&nbsp;Check for flowering and seed development to prevent seed set, spread and build up of seed bank<br/>
    -&nbsp;During the rainy season, regularly cut the weeds before flowering. Either chop plants into pieces and heap them within the hedge to dry and decompose of feed to livestock<br/>-&nbsp;Hand-pull and destroy the weeds emerging in the crop field, ensuring the taproot is severed well below the crown to prevent regrowth. Allow them to  dry and decompose or feed them to livestock<br/>-&nbsp;Do not slash the plants, as plants can regrow from cut fragments<br/>-&nbsp;Consistent follow-up work is required for sustainable management<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Tithonia diversifolia</i>

Title: Blackjack
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Purchase and sow certified crop and pasture seed to reduce the chance of contamination<br/>-&nbsp;Seeds are spread by animals - do not allow livestock into an affected area to avoid spread<br/>-&nbsp;Seeds are spread on clothes and shoes - remove seeds when leaving an infested area<br/>-&nbsp;Species is spread by water - use only water from sources free of the weed and avoid runoff from surrounding infested fields<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid overgrazing to prevent establishment of the weed in disturbed patches<br/>-&nbsp;Prevent spread of the weed by slashing or mowing wfhen they are still small<br/>-&nbsp;A maize-bean intercropping system can effectively suppress the weed<br/>-&nbsp;Use thick mulch with dry non-weedy plant materials to prevent germination of the weed<br/>
    -&nbsp;Annual erect broadleaved weed (up to 1 m high) with green composed leaves and a strong taproot<br/>-&nbsp;White flowers with yellow centre and 1 cm long black seeds with 2-4 yellowish awns at one end<br/>-&nbsp;Check fields and surroundings for presence of weed once a week during early crop establishment, and once a month later<br/>-&nbsp;Check for flowering and seed development to prevent seed set, spread and build up of seed bank<br/>
    -&nbsp;Dig up or hand pull plants prior to onset of flowering, ensuring the taproot is severed well below the crown to prevent regrowth<br/>-&nbsp;Mow or slash the species prior to flowering and seed set<br/>-&nbsp;A no-seed threshold is important as it prevents further spread of the weed<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Bidens pilosa</i>

Title: Bitter vine
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Avoid heavy grazing and browsing since the weed thrives in open, disturbed areas<br/>-&nbsp;As seeds can travel on animals and machinery, do not allow animals into an affected area, limit machinery use in the area and keep machinery clean to prevent spread<br/>
    -&nbsp;Vigorous, fast-growing, perennial, creeping or twining plant<br/>-&nbsp;Flowers: arranged in large, loose heads; white or cream; produce many seeds<br/>-&nbsp;Stems: herbaceous to semi-woody; ribbed; slighty hairy<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: smooth; triangular; toothed; 6-15 cm long, 3-9 cm wide<br/>-&nbsp;Look along waterways and transport routes. Seeds disperse via water, wind animals and vehicles<br/>-&nbsp;Similar species include <i>Ageratina adenophora</i>, <i>A. riparia</i>, <Mikania cordata</i> and <i>M. scandens</i><br/>
    -&nbsp;For rubber plantations: graze fields with sheep  they will eat the weed first and leguminous ground cover last<br/>-&nbsp;Allow a herb layer to establish in managed orchards, forests or perennial crop fields (such as tea) in order to shade out weed<br/>-&nbsp;Cut near ground once a month for three consecutive months in summer and autumn, and then in winter and spring  this can eliminate 90% of weed<br/>-&nbsp;Hand-pull prior to seed maturity or during the rainy season<br/>-&nbsp;Sickle weed before seed sets  this gives temporary control. Weed regrows quickly from cut stumps<br/>-&nbsp;Uproot before flowering and fruiting<br/>-&nbsp;Do not leave weed fragments on the ground  they can take root and establish as new plants<br/>-&nbsp;If available in your country, use the biocontrol agent, <i>Puccinia spegazzinii</i>, a rust to control the weed<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Mikania micrantha</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108258.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108259.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108260.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Avoid heavy grazing and browsing since the weed thrives in open, disturbed areas<br/>-&nbsp;As seeds can travel on animals and machinery, do not allow animals into an affected area, limit machinery use in the area and keep machinery clean to prevent spread<br/> -&nbsp;Vigorous, fast-growing, perennial, creeping or twining plant<br/>-&nbsp;Flowers: arranged in large, loose heads; white or cream; produce many seeds<br/>-&nbsp;Stems: herbaceous to semi-woody; ribbed; slighty hairy<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves: smooth; triangular; toothed; 6-15 cm long, 3-9 cm wide<br/>-&nbsp;Look along waterways and transport routes. Title: Whiteflies on tomato
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Remove tomato residues and volunteer tomatoes and bury or burn<br/>-&nbsp;Plant varieties which are resistant to whitefly and the viruses it can transmit, if available<br/>-&nbsp;Plant tomatoes 0.5-2 feet apart with at least 3 feet between rows<br/>-&nbsp;Use a shade net (size 400 m) / tunnel for 3-5 weeks while in nursery to prevent attack by white flies<br/>-&nbsp;Transplant shortly after or during the rainy season<br/>-&nbsp;Use white/black inert plastic mulch 10-20 days after transplanting which attracts and kills white flies<br/>-&nbsp;Conserve natural enemies (wasps, ladybirds) by mulching or leaving a natural hedge of flowering plants (e.g. <i>Tithonia</i>, <i>Lantana</i>) along the edges to offer them refuge<br/>-&nbsp;Plant repellent crops such as coriander or Mexican marigold after 3-4 rows of tomato to repel the whiteflies<br/>-&nbsp;Keep the field weed-free throughout the cropping season since weeds act as alternate hosts and can harbour whitefly-transmitted viruses<br/>
    -&nbsp;Adult: A sucking insect, about 1 mm long. The body and wings are covered in a powdery, waxy secretion, white to slightly yellowish in colour<br/>-&nbsp;Larva: yellow-white scales, 0.3-0.6 mm long<br/>-&nbsp;Puparium: flat, irregular, oval shape, 0.7 mm long<br/>-&nbsp;After transplanting monitor weekly until harvest<br/>-&nbsp;Search for leaves with whitefly groups (they are mainly on underside of leaves). They will fly when you shake plants.<br/>-&nbsp;Yellowing, curling, crumpling of leaves occurs at heavy infestation.<br/>-&nbsp;Whiteflies can transmit viruses which cause virus disease. Leaves will have yellowish green mosaics<br/>-&nbsp;Action threshold level is 10-20 plants with symptoms and/or at least 20 whiteflies on at least 10-20 plants/acre <br/>
    -&nbsp;Use yellow sticky traps at about 1 per 5 plants.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray biopesticide <i>Beauveria</i> (check product labels for dosage and application timing). After 21 to 28 days, next application if needed. Another entomopathogenic fungi that white fly is suscptible too is <i>Lecanicillium lecanii</i><br/>-&nbsp;Use hot pepper spray at rate of 30 chopped peppers in warm water. Soak for 1 day, dilute in 10 L water and add 20 drops of liquid soap before spraying<br/>-&nbsp;Spray Azadirachtin 0.03% (50 ml/20L water) 15 days after transplant  <br/>-&nbsp;Use liquid soap spray at rate of 3 table spoons per litre of water<br/>-&nbsp;Conserve natural enemies. Several wasps, including species in the <i>Encarsia</i> and <i>Eretmocerus</i> genera, parasitize whiteflies. Whitefly nymphs are also preyed upon by bigeyed bugs, lacewing larvae, and ladybird larvae.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Bemisia tabaci</i> 
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108807.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108808.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108809.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Remove tomato residues and volunteer tomatoes and bury or burn<br/>-&nbsp;Plant varieties which are resistant to whitefly and the viruses it can transmit, if available<br/>-&nbsp;Plant tomatoes 0.5-2 feet apart with at least 3 feet between rows<br/>-&nbsp;Use a shade net (size 400 m) / tunnel for 3-5 weeks while in nursery to prevent attack by white flies<br/>-&nbsp;Transplant shortly after or during the rainy season<br/>-&nbsp;Use white/black inert plastic mulch 10-20 days after transplanting which attracts and kills white flies<br/>-&nbsp;Conserve natural enemies (wasps, ladybirds) by mulching or leaving a natural hedge of flowering plants (e.g. <i>Tithonia</i>, <i>Lantana</i>) along the edges to offer them refuge<br/>-&nbsp;Plant repellent crops such as coriander or Mexican marigold after 3-4 rows of tomato to repel the whiteflies<br/>-&nbsp;Keep the field weed-free throughout the cropping season since weeds act as alternate hosts and can harbour whitefly-transmitted viruses<br/> -&nbsp;Adult: A sucking insect, about 1 mm long. Whitefly nymphs are also preyed upon by bigeyed bugs, lacewing larvae, and ladybird larvae.<br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Termites on cassava
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Avoid planting in termite infested fields if possible<br/>-&nbsp;Plough termite mounds prior to sowing to destroy the termite queen and expose the termites to predators (e.g. ants, dragonflies, ground beetles, bats, birds, frogs).<br/>-&nbsp;Plant cuttings at a slightly higher rate to account for losses due to termite damage<br/>-&nbsp;Deep planting of transplants/cuttings<br/>-&nbsp;Remove plant debris around the plant basin/hole before planting since plant debris attracts termites<br/>-&nbsp;Apply wood ashes around the plant in the planting basin/hole to repel termites<br/>-&nbsp;Use optimal irrigation and fertlization rates. Ensure development of strong healthy plants that can tolerate some termite damage.<br/>-&nbsp;Ensure there is plenty of soil humus for termites to feed on instead of crop - avoid bare, dry disturbed, organic-deficient, residue-free soil<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid importing termite infested materials into fields<br/>-&nbsp;Plant cassava cuttings on ridges so they are less likely to be attacked by termites<br/>-&nbsp;Plant cassava cuttings when rains become steady. Termites prefer a dry environment.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant basil (<i>Ocimum</i> spp.) as a barrier to repel termites.  Leave space between basil and crop because this native plant may reduce growth/survival of cassava if grown too close.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Additional relevant crops: maize, yam, rice, sorghum, tomato, peppers, groundnut<br/>-&nbsp;Scout for the presence of termites once a week<br/>-&nbsp;Monitor the field more frequently (twice a week) during dry spells. Most termite damage occurs during dry periods.<br/>-&nbsp;Look for termite nests/mounds in the field and galleries in soil and wood. Termites build nests above and/or below ground depending on the species. Mound structures can vary and be up to 5 m high.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults: ant-like, mostly wingless, 0.5-1 cm long<br/>-&nbsp;The difference between termites and ants is that termites have straight antenna, broad waist, and if wings are present then forewings and hindwings are of the same size and have many veins<br/>-&nbsp;Look for chewed stem cuttings. These grow poorly, die and rot. Termites may even enter the stems, weakening them and causing them to break.<br/>-&nbsp;Consider using direct measures if 5-10 plants are affected in 1 field and if the crop is still far from maturing<br/>
    -&nbsp;Manually destroy termite nest. Find the queen and destroy it (often deep in the nest). Pour boiling water on the nest or burn plant debris to kill the rest.<br/>-&nbsp;Burn plant residues on top of a termite mound to suffocate them - this is not a long-term control method as the queen will probably not die<br/>-&nbsp;Hand tilling or flooding the termite runways/tunnels will also offer temporary control<br/>-&nbsp;Spray crop with extract of neem leaves or neem fruits and apply wood ashes around the plant. Use 1kg crushed neem leaves and/or fruit in 5L water, and 2 handfuls of ash per plant.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray crop with extract of <i>Lantana camara</i> (Tusepo) leaves. Boil 2 kg crushed leaves in 5L for 30 minutes.<br/>-&nbsp;Use <i>Beauveria bassiana</i> as biological control if available in your country. Bring to boil one teacup of mature seeds for 5 minutes. Allow to cool and then strain to get a clear liquid. Add 1 litre of water.<br/>-&nbsp;Harvest as soon as possible. Termite damage gets worse the closer it gets to harvest.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect and bury/burn plant debris after harvest<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Coptotermes</i> spp., <i>Macrotermes</i> spp., <i>Odontotermes spp.</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108818.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108819.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Avoid planting in termite infested fields if possible<br/>-&nbsp;Plough termite mounds prior to sowing to destroy the termite queen and expose the termites to predators (e.g. ants, dragonflies, ground beetles, bats, birds, frogs).<br/>-&nbsp;Plant cuttings at a slightly higher rate to account for losses due to termite damage<br/>-&nbsp;Deep planting of transplants/cuttings<br/>-&nbsp;Remove plant debris around the plant basin/hole before planting since plant debris attracts termites<br/>-&nbsp;Apply wood ashes around the plant in the planting basin/hole to repel termites<br/>-&nbsp;Use optimal irrigation and fertlization rates. Title: Fruit fly on tomato
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Bag immature fruits with polythene, paper or silk clothing within one month of flowering<br/>-&nbsp;Timely planting to avoid tomato ripening and harvesting period during ripening seasons of other fruit hosts (see below). Otherwise flies may migrate from the fruits of these trees into tomato fields.<br/>-&nbsp;In order to prevent the spread of the insect pest, avoid rotating or planting near alternative hosts; guava, chillies, citrus, melons, avocado, pear, mango, banana, pepper, wild fruits<br/>-&nbsp;Expose pupae to sun by raking up soil surrounding tomato plants<br/>-&nbsp;Harvest fruits at physiological maturity before they become very ripe and soft which makes it easy for the flies to lay eggs in them<br/>
    -&nbsp;Tomato fruits rot when larvae of fruit flies live and feed inside.<br/>-&nbsp;The fly adult is a small winged insect of nearly 1 cm in length. It is like a house fly in shape but smaller, and its body can be colourful. Look for adult flies on the flowers and fruits.<br/>-&nbsp;For early detection, use commercial pheromone traps with trimedlure (TML), or cue lure (CL), or methyl eugenol (ME) attractants (3 traps/ km2). Follow infestation regularly, once flies are detected.<br/>-&nbsp;Inspect fruits for soft black spotted ovi-punctures on the fruits weekly. Larvae enter the tomato and are yellowish whitish, 0.5 to 1 cm long, 2 to 3 mm thick.<br/>-&nbsp;When damage is detected on 1 to 2 tomatoes/ 10 plants on average, consider act quickly before more eggs are laid and hatching larvae feed inside tomatoes<br/>-&nbsp;At high infestations, i.e. in case of mass invasion, your tomatoes are likely to be lost. Then no action is needed<br/>
    -&nbsp;Apply neem oil or neem seed extract to kill pupating larvae<br/>-&nbsp;Grind 50 g wild basil leaves; soak overnight in 2-3 litres water; strain; add 10g soap; then spray over un-ripened tomatoes (reach coverage) early in the mornings.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect all fallen fruits from below nearby fruit trees because they host many fruit fly larvae. Place in black polythene bags, tie them up and expose to sun or bury infested fruits more than 50 cm deep in soil in order to kill larvae<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Bactrocera aquilonis, B. atrisetosa, B. cucumis, B. dorsalis, B. tryoni, B. dorsalis, B. latifrons</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108952.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_108953.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Bag immature fruits with polythene, paper or silk clothing within one month of flowering<br/>-&nbsp;Timely planting to avoid tomato ripening and harvesting period during ripening seasons of other fruit hosts (see below). Otherwise flies may migrate from the fruits of these trees into tomato fields.<br/>-&nbsp;In order to prevent the spread of the insect pest, avoid rotating or planting near alternative hosts; guava, chillies, citrus, melons, avocado, pear, mango, banana, pepper, wild fruits<br/>-&nbsp;Expose pupae to sun by raking up soil surrounding tomato plants<br/>-&nbsp;Harvest fruits at physiological maturity before they become very ripe and soft which makes it easy for the flies to lay eggs in them<br/> -&nbsp;Tomato fruits rot when larvae of fruit flies live and feed inside.<br/>-&nbsp;The fly adult is a small winged insect of nearly 1 cm in length. It is like a house fly in shape but smaller, and its body can be colourful. Look for adult flies on the flowers and fruits.<br/>-&nbsp;For early detection, use commercial pheromone traps with trimedlure (TML), or cue lure (CL), or methyl eugenol (ME) attractants (3 traps/ km2). Follow infestation regularly, once flies are detected.<br/>-&nbsp;Inspect fruits for soft black spotted ovi-punctures on the fruits weekly. Larvae enter the tomato and are yellowish whitish, 0.5 to 1 cm long, 2 to 3 mm thick.<br/>-&nbsp;When damage is detected on 1 to 2 tomatoes/ 10 plants on average, consider act quickly before more eggs are laid and hatching larvae feed inside tomatoes<br/>-&nbsp;At high infestations, i.e. in case of mass invasion, your tomatoes are likely to be lost. Then no action is needed<br/> -&nbsp;Apply neem oil or neem seed extract to kill pupating larvae<br/>-&nbsp;Grind 50 g wild basil leaves; soak overnight in 2-3 litres water; strain; add 10g soap; then spray over un-ripened tomatoes (reach coverage) early in the mornings.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect all fallen fruits from below nearby fruit trees because they host many fruit fly larvae. Place in black polythene bags, tie them up and expose to sun or bury infested fruits more than 50 cm deep in soil in order to kill larvae<br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: American Cotton Bollworm
Authors:   Mr. Vijay Thokre, Mr. Nilesh Patkar, Dr. Malarvanan, Mr Rajkumar
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Deep summer ploughing<br/>-&nbsp;Resistant variety (AKT)<br/>-&nbsp;Early sowing<br/>-&nbsp;Remove all the collateral and alternate hosts<br/>-&nbsp;1kg of fenugreek powder in 1 litre of water soaked overnight. The next morning, take out the filterate and add 40 litres of water, then spray.<br/>-&nbsp;Synchronized sowing with short duration varieties.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid continuous cropping and rattooning.<br/>-&nbsp;Grow the less-preferred crops like green gram, black gram, soybean, castor, sorghum, etc along the border.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove and destroy crop residues immediately after harvest.<br/>-&nbsp;Optimal use and split application of N fertilizers.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoiding extended crop growth by continuouus irrigation.<br/>-&nbsp;Sow trap crop of marigold plants<br/>
    -&nbsp;Pheromone traps 4 to 8 per acre.<br/>-&nbsp;Light traps 2 per acre.<br/>-&nbsp;1 larvae/ plant or 5% damage to fruiting bodies.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Bird perches 8 to 10 / acre.<br/>-&nbsp;Release <i>Trichogramma chilonis</i> 2 to 3 cards per acre.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray 5% neem seed kernel extract at flowering stage.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray 3 times with HaNPV (Helicoverpa armigera Nuclear Polyhedrosis Virus) at 3 trillion POB / ha in evening hours at 7th and 12th week after sowing. Mixing 10% jaggery (adjuvant) is advisable. <br/>-&nbsp;Hand pick & destroy various insect stages, affected plant parts and rosetted flowers. <br/>-&nbsp;Spray entomopathogenic fungi such as <i>Metarhizium anisopliae, Beauveria bassiana.</i><br/>-&nbsp;Release of Chrysoperla carnea @ 1,00,000/ha at 6th, 13th and 14th week after sowing.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use of personal protective equipment (PPEs) such as gloves, mask, goggles and keeping antidotes are mandatory by the spray applicators<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spinosad 48 SC 50 - 75 or.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class III (slightly hazardous)<br/>-&nbsp;Chlorpyriphos 20 EC 500  700 during fruiting stages .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazadardous)<br/>-&nbsp;Neem products ( 1500 ppm ) 2.5 litre 40  60 day .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Botanical pesticide; unlikely to present acute hazard in normal use<br/>-&nbsp;Quinalphos 20 A F 500 - 700 during fruiting stages or.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazadardous)<br/>-&nbsp;Profenofos 50 EC 1000 - 1250 during fruiting stages.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazadardous)<br/>
Scientific Names: kai thulaippan

Title: Common rust of Maize
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;<b>Use resistant varieties if available</b><br/>-&nbsp;Use clean maize seed which is from a reliable source<br/>-&nbsp;Practise deep ploughing of crop residues (to at least 50 cm) if there is previous history of common rust in the field<br/>-&nbsp;Practise crop rotation with non-cereal crops at least every 2-3 years (e.g. beans, fababean, chickpea).<br/>-&nbsp;Remove and destroy weeds which are <i>Oxalis</i> spp. or <i> Zea</i> spp. as these are alternative hosts<br/>-&nbsp;Plant maize in rows in the direction of prevailing winds for maximum air circulation<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor the plant every week, especially when there are favourable environmental conditions (Mild (16-25 C) and humid conditions (>95%) favour rust development)<br/>-&nbsp;Start to observe the maize plant after 6 leaves emerge until the tasseling stage<br/>-&nbsp;In early stages, small chlorotic areas develop on leaves. Later, small, elongated, scattered and dark red-brownish pustules form on both the upper and lower sides of the leaf<br/>-&nbsp;Pustules may also appear on husks, tassels and stalks<br/>-&nbsp;If the infection is severe then leaves and leaf sheaths turn yellow and die early<br/>
    -&nbsp;Remove lower leaves which are infested to improve air circulation and reduce the innoculum (traces of fungi). Destroy the infected leaves by burning or burying deeply (at least 50 cm)<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Puccinia sorghi</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109250.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109251.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109252.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;<b>Use resistant varieties if available</b><br/>-&nbsp;Use clean maize seed which is from a reliable source<br/>-&nbsp;Practise deep ploughing of crop residues (to at least 50 cm) if there is previous history of common rust in the field<br/>-&nbsp;Practise crop rotation with non-cereal crops at least every 2-3 years (e.g. beans, fababean, chickpea).<br/>-&nbsp;Remove and destroy weeds which are <i>Oxalis</i> spp. or <i> Zea</i> spp. as these are alternative hosts<br/>-&nbsp;Plant maize in rows in the direction of prevailing winds for maximum air circulation<br/> -&nbsp;Monitor the plant every week, especially when there are favourable environmental conditions (Mild (16-25 C) and humid conditions (>95%) favour rust development)<br/>-&nbsp;Start to observe the maize plant after 6 leaves emerge until the tasseling stage<br/>-&nbsp;In early stages, small chlorotic areas develop on leaves. Title: Bean rust
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Plant resistant varieties if available<br/>-&nbsp;Use clean bean seeds originating from non-diseased plants or from certified seed dealers<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting new bean crop next to an old bean field since the fungus is spread by the wind and by water (splashing)<br/>-&nbsp;Keep field weed free. Be sure to remove alternate host plants e.g. <i>Oxalis</i> spp. and volunteer bean plants<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid walking through the field during wet weather to prevent disease spread from one plant to another<br/>-&nbsp;Carry out mixed intercropping with maize which blocks the spread of fungal spores between bean plants<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid long periods of leaf wetness by carefully selecting planting dates and avoiding watering at night when temperatures are cooler and less evaporation happens<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid over application of nitrogen (delays plant maturity) and ensure adequate potassium fertilization to promote healthy growth<br/>-&nbsp;Maximise air flow around the plants by spacing plants well apart (e.g. plant bush bean rows 60-90 cm apart; plant single seeds within rows 5-10 cm apart, or 20 cm apart if planting two seeds per stand) and avoiding damp humid sites<br/>-&nbsp;Practise two year crop rotation with non-leguminous plants such as tomatoes, cabbages and kales<br/>
    -&nbsp;Additional relevant crops: peas, sweet pea, other legumes<br/>-&nbsp;Monitor beans particularly closely during blossom and early pod development<br/>-&nbsp;Early symptoms are small, slightly raised white to pale yellow spots on the upper and/or lower leaf surfaces <br/>-&nbsp;The white/pale yellow spots enlarge and become reddish-brown powdery spots, about 3 mm wide. These spots may be surrounded by a yellow halo-like border<br/>-&nbsp;Also look out for spots on the petioles, pods and the stem.<br/>-&nbsp;Leaves may curl up, dry up, turn brown and drop off prematurely if the infestation is severe<br/>-&nbsp;Pod set, pod fill and seed size can be reduced<br/>-&nbsp;Spots may eventually turn brownish-black<br/>-&nbsp;Bean rust can be distinguished from leaf spots caused by blights and bronzing by the fact that the reddish-brown powdery spots will rub off on your finger<br/>-&nbsp;Infections that occur at or after pod bump and stripe don't usually cause significant economic loss, so control is less essential<br/>
    -&nbsp;Pick affected leaves, take away from the field in a plastic bag and destroy by burning (if allowed in your area) as soon as possible to prevent spores from spreading<br/>-&nbsp;After harvest, remove and burn old crop residues since the disease can live for one year on the material left in the field<br/>-&nbsp;If infestation is severe, deep plough field to about 45 cm to turn any remaining residues into the soil. This will encourage the plant remains to rot and reduce the amount of fungal disease carried over to the next bean season<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Uromyces appendiculatus</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109566.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109567.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109568.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Plant resistant varieties if available<br/>-&nbsp;Use clean bean seeds originating from non-diseased plants or from certified seed dealers<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting new bean crop next to an old bean field since the fungus is spread by the wind and by water (splashing)<br/>-&nbsp;Keep field weed free. Title: Blossom end rot on tomato
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Harden off seedlings for 2 weeks before planting (slowly expose them to the outdoors). This will make them more resistant to blossom end rot<br/>-&nbsp;Test levels of calcium in soil and acidity levels (Soil pH <=5.5). Acidic soil prevents plants taking up much calcium. Test soil 1-2 months before planting<br/>-&nbsp;Apply calcium based fertilizers to the soil such as calcium nitrate during vegetative and flowering stage at a rate based on the soil test results. <br/>-&nbsp;In acidic soils, apply lime (based on soil test results) to increase pH levels to enhance mobility of the calcium nutrient and uptake<br/>-&nbsp;If no mineral fertilisers are available, apply ground/crushed egg shells to soil (12g of egg-shells/plant) or apply bone meal (250g/hill) in the planting hole<br/>-&nbsp;Use less N and more P (+ obviously Ca). Rapid growth induced by high N can contribute to blossom end rot<br/>-&nbsp;Mulch using maize stover, wheat straw or dried grass to conserve soil moisture, especially during the flowering and fruiting stage<br/>-&nbsp;Do not over-irrigate plants and apply sufficient amount of water to keep the soil moist throughout the cropping period<br/>
    -&nbsp;Blossom end rot is not a disease; it is a symptom of calcium deficiency in the soil and therefore in the plant.<br/>-&nbsp;During every watering session check for end rot symptoms on the plants, or check plants at least once a week. The first fruits are most severely affected.<br/>-&nbsp;Check on the young leaves for scorching and curling downwards, darkening of growing points of the tomato plants<br/>-&nbsp;Check for sunken leathery brownish or dark patches at the bottom end of the tomato fruit (blossom end) during fruit set and fruit development<br/>-&nbsp;In some varieties, check for cracks at the base of the fruit during every watering session<br/>-&nbsp;Consider action as soon as you notice the presence of sunburn like damage on the leaves and curling downwards, darkening of growing points or sunken leathery brownish or dark patched bottom end of the tomato fruit in at least two plants <br/>-&nbsp;Carry out soil analysis annually to determine calcium levels in soil. Send sample off to a certified laboratory<br/>-&nbsp;Test the soil moisture by pressing soil between fingers. If the soil particles do not stick together the soil is too dry and the field should be watered.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Remove fruits that have this disorder to avoid secondary infections<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: Calcium deficiency
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109569.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109570.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_109571.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Harden off seedlings for 2 weeks before planting (slowly expose them to the outdoors). This will make them more resistant to blossom end rot<br/>-&nbsp;Test levels of calcium in soil and acidity levels (Soil pH <=5.5). Acidic soil prevents plants taking up much calcium. Test soil 1-2 months before planting<br/>-&nbsp;Apply calcium based fertilizers to the soil such as calcium nitrate during vegetative and flowering stage at a rate based on the soil test results. <br/>-&nbsp;In acidic soils, apply lime (based on soil test results) to increase pH levels to enhance mobility of the calcium nutrient and uptake<br/>-&nbsp;If no mineral fertilisers are available, apply ground/crushed egg shells to soil (12g of egg-shells/plant) or apply bone meal (250g/hill) in the planting hole<br/>-&nbsp;Use less N and more P (+ obviously Ca).
