Title: Fruit and shoot borer of Okra 
Authors:   Arun Khajuria, Sushil Kumar, Vijay Koushal, Rajiv Singh
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Remove and destroy affected fruits and shoots; for shoots by

nipping them off below pest hole.

<br/>-&nbsp;Grow carrot, sunflower and buckwheat to attract pest away from okra.<br/>-&nbsp;Cover beds with polythene sheet 45 gauge (0.45 mm) thickness for three weeks before sowing, this will reduce the pest in the soil.<br/>-&nbsp;Use pheromone traps  (5-8 per acre), put at least one foot above the crop canopy.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid growing Malvaceous crops (cotton, hollyhock ) in sequence.<br/>-&nbsp;Deep plough fields during summer.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Look for withered and drooping terminal shoots, shedding of buds and flowers, bore holes in fruits and deformed fruits.<br/>-&nbsp;Look for blue eggs on the underside of leaves and brown, boat shaped larvae in terminal shoots and fruits.<br/>-&nbsp;Set up pheromone traps (3-5 per acre). If 2-4 silvery green adults are caught per trap then consider direct control actions.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Release egg parasitoid <i>Trichogramma chilonis</i> at a rate of 40,000 per acre. 

<br/>-&nbsp;Apply neem cake at 100 kg/acre at the time of transplanting.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray azadirachtin 0.03% (300 ppm) as neem oil at a rate of 1000-2000 ml in 200-400 litres of water per acre.<br/>-&nbsp;Apply azadirachtin 5% w/w neem extract concentrate at 80 ml in 160 litres of water per acre<br/>-&nbsp;Conserve predators of the borer, such as coccinellids, parasitic wasps, dragonflies, spiders, robber flies, reduviid bugs, praying mantis, fire ants, earwings and ground beetles.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use personnel protection equipments (PPE)

while using chemical pesticides<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray crop with chloranthraniliprole 18.5% SC (50 ml in 200 L of water/acre).

.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class U (unlikely to present acute hazard).<br/>-&nbsp;Cypermethrin 25% EC (60-80 ml in 200 L of water/acre).&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Deltamethrin 2.8% EC (160-240 ml in 160-240 L of water/acre).&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;During flowering stage spray with Spinosad (2 ml/litre of water). .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class III (slightly hazardous).<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Earias vitella</i> (Fabricius) (Lepidoptera Noctuidae); <i>E. insulana</i> (Boisduval) (Lepidoptera Noctuidae)
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_110876.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_110877.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Remove and destroy affected fruits and shoots; for shoots by

nipping them off below pest hole.

<br/>-&nbsp;Grow carrot, sunflower and buckwheat to attract pest away from okra.<br/>-&nbsp;Cover beds with polythene sheet 45 gauge (0.45 mm) thickness for three weeks before sowing, this will reduce the pest in the soil.<br/>-&nbsp;Use pheromone traps  (5-8 per acre), put at least one foot above the crop canopy.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid growing Malvaceous crops (cotton, hollyhock ) in sequence.<br/>-&nbsp;Deep plough fields during summer.<br/> -&nbsp;Look for withered and drooping terminal shoots, shedding of buds and flowers, bore holes in fruits and deformed fruits.<br/>-&nbsp;Look for blue eggs on the underside of leaves and brown, boat shaped larvae in terminal shoots and fruits.<br/>-&nbsp;Set up pheromone traps (3-5 per acre). If 2-4 silvery green adults are caught per trap then consider direct control actions.<br/> -&nbsp;Release egg parasitoid <i>Trichogramma chilonis</i> at a rate of 40,000 per acre. 

<br/>-&nbsp;Apply neem cake at 100 kg/acre at the time of transplanting.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray azadirachtin 0.03% (300 ppm) as neem oil at a rate of 1000-2000 ml in 200-400 litres of water per acre.<br/>-&nbsp;Apply azadirachtin 5% w/w neem extract concentrate at 80 ml in 160 litres of water per acre<br/>-&nbsp;Conserve predators of the borer, such as coccinellids, parasitic wasps, dragonflies, spiders, robber flies, reduviid bugs, praying mantis, fire ants, earwings and ground beetles.<br/> -&nbsp;Use personnel protection equipments (PPE)

while using chemical pesticides<br/> -&nbsp;Spray crop with chloranthraniliprole 18.5% SC (50 ml in 200 L of water/acre).

.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class U (unlikely to present acute hazard).<br/>-&nbsp;Cypermethrin 25% EC (60-80 ml in 200 L of water/acre).&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Deltamethrin 2.8% EC (160-240 ml in 160-240 L of water/acre).&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;During flowering stage spray with Spinosad (2 ml/litre of water). .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class III (slightly hazardous).<br/>

Title: Sheath blight of Rice
Authors:   Manmoshan Sharma, Arun Khajuria, Sushil Kumar
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Good field sanitation before and during season, including clearing plant debris from the previous harvest.<br/>-&nbsp;Deep summer ploughing to expose disease to sun; good drainage.<br/>-&nbsp;Use tolerant varieties: 1121, Pusa Basmati 1509.<br/>-&nbsp;Fertilize based on soil health card.<br/>-&nbsp;Soil application of <i>Pseudomonas fluorescens</i> (2.5 kg/ha with 100 kg of FYM at field preparation).<br/>-&nbsp;Treat seed with Carbendazim at a rate of 2 g/kg of seed (see restrictions).  <br/>-&nbsp;Avoid close planting.<br/>-&nbsp;Sow short duration varieties such as IET-1410, RATNA, PC-19.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Check for brownish, irregular semi circles on sheath, stem and leaves. <br/>-&nbsp;Check for whitish cottony growth (mycelium) on leaf sheath/blade near the water line. <br/>-&nbsp;Check for minute ball like structure (sclerotia) on leaf sheath near the water line.<br/>-&nbsp;Be extra vigilent 30 to 35 days after transplanting and onward.<br/>-&nbsp;If around 5% of plants show symptoms use then consider direct control actions.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Foliar application of <i>Pseudomonas fluorescens</i> at a rate of 2.5 kg/Ha with 500 L of water when threshold reached. <br/>-&nbsp;Drain off standing water from the infected field.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use of personal protective equipment (PPE) is recommended while using chemical pesticides.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray with Carbendazim at a rate of 0.1% at appearance of disease or 250-300 gm/acre with 150 litres of water.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO U (unlikely to present acute hazard in normal use). Foliar application. Maximum of two applications with 15 day interval. Use at maximum tillering stage. Be careful as can kill important pollinators such as honeybees.<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Rhizoctonia solani</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_110878.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_110879.img?width=800&height=640
Content:

Title: Stripe rust of wheat
Authors:   Dr. Vishal Gupta
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Sow resistant varieties for irrigated/unirrigated areas.<br/>-&nbsp;Timely sowing (first week of November).<br/>-&nbsp;Seed treatment with tebucinazole (2 g/kg of seed).<br/>-&nbsp;Apply balanced dose of NPK.<br/>-&nbsp;Promote cultivation of resistant varieties for fodder purpose.<br/>-&nbsp;Seed replacement every 3-4 years.<br/>-&nbsp;Sowing of mosaic of 2-3 varieties at village level.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid promotion of cultivation of a single variety on larger areas.<br/>-&nbsp;Strictly follow irrigation schedule. <br/>-&nbsp;Cleaning of clothes and shoes after visiting infected field.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Look for yellow stripes/turmeric powder (spores) on flag leaves (upper most); if there is no powder it may be spot blotch or a nutritional deficiency.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake strict monitoring in water logged areas.<br/>-&nbsp;Monitoring after 60 days (last week of December) in irrigated areas and after 30 days in late sown areas of wheat.  <br/>-&nbsp;Be more vigilant when dew formation takes place.<br/>-&nbsp;Share monitoring information at community level, village level, district level and state level.<br/>-&nbsp;Check crops three times a week from January onwards.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Foliar application of propiconazole or tebuconazole at a rate of 1 ml/L of water on the appearance of symptom(s).&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Crop raised for seed purpose, apply  foliar application of Propiconazole or Tebuconazole (1 ml/L of water during first week of February). .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazardous). Apply at tillering stage and repeat spray after 15-day interval. However avoid spraying during rains.<br/>-&nbsp;Tilt/Folicur (Trizoles), Systemic/ FRAC.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;May harm aquatic organisms.<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Puccinia striiformis</i> f. sp. <i>tritici</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_110880.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_110881.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Sow resistant varieties for irrigated/unirrigated areas.<br/>-&nbsp;Timely sowing (first week of November).<br/>-&nbsp;Seed treatment with tebucinazole (2 g/kg of seed).<br/>-&nbsp;Apply balanced dose of NPK.<br/>-&nbsp;Promote cultivation of resistant varieties for fodder purpose.<br/>-&nbsp;Seed replacement every 3-4 years.<br/>-&nbsp;Sowing of mosaic of 2-3 varieties at village level.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid promotion of cultivation of a single variety on larger areas.<br/>-&nbsp;Strictly follow irrigation schedule. <br/>-&nbsp;Cleaning of clothes and shoes after visiting infected field.<br/> -&nbsp;Look for yellow stripes/turmeric powder (spores) on flag leaves (upper most); if there is no powder it may be spot blotch or a nutritional deficiency.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake strict monitoring in water logged areas.<br/>-&nbsp;Monitoring after 60 days (last week of December) in irrigated areas and after 30 days in late sown areas of wheat.  <br/>-&nbsp;Be more vigilant when dew formation takes place.<br/>-&nbsp;Share monitoring information at community level, village level, district level and state level.<br/>-&nbsp;Check crops three times a week from January onwards.<br/> -&nbsp;Foliar application of propiconazole or tebuconazole at a rate of 1 ml/L of water on the appearance of symptom(s).&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Crop raised for seed purpose, apply  foliar application of Propiconazole or Tebuconazole (1 ml/L of water during first week of February). .&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO class II (moderately hazardous). Apply at tillering stage and repeat spray after 15-day interval. However avoid spraying during rains.<br/>-&nbsp;Tilt/Folicur (Trizoles), Systemic/ FRAC.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;May harm aquatic organisms.<br/>

Title: Tomato fruit borer
Authors:   Dr. Kamlesh Bali Sr. Scientist, (Sher-e-Kashmir University of Agriculture Sciences-Jammu) Sushil Kumar and Arun Khajuria( Plant Health Clinic Department of Agriculture Jammu)
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Deep summer ploughing.<br/>-&nbsp;Early planting can help avoid this pest.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid monocropping. Grow simultaneously 40 day-old African marigold with 25 day-old tomato seedlings, in the 1:16 rows.<br/>-&nbsp;Correctly space your seeds/seedlings. Spacing depends upon variety but in general should be 60 x 45 cm.<br/>-&nbsp;Install bird perches in the field to encourage predatory birds.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural pest enemies (such as spiders, ants, parasitic wasps and robber flies).<br/>
    -&nbsp;Regular monitoring of the crop to check for caterpillars on the leaves, ideally during morning and evening hours. Threshold is 5-10% of leaves infested.<br/>-&nbsp;Use pheromone and/or light traps to check for the presence of the adult moths. Use 1 trap per acre. Action threshold is 3 or more adult moths in a trap.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Install pheromomone traps (5 traps per acre) for mass trapping of male moths. Change the lure every 15 days.<br/>-&nbsp;Install light traps to attract and kill adult moths.<br/>-&nbsp;Release the egg parasitoid <i>Trichogramma brassilences</i> or <i>T. pretiosum</i> at a rate of 40000-60000 parasitoids per acre once the action threshold is reached, or at the time of flowering. Repeat at 15-day intervals.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray neem-based insecticides e.g. NSKE (5% or Neem oil at a rate of 3 ml/L of water).<br/>-&nbsp;Foliar spray of the virus HaNPV (250 LE/Ha) with 20 Jaggery in the evening hours.<br/>-&nbsp;Foliar spray of the bacterium <i>Bacillus thuringiensis</i> var. kurstaki (1-1.5 kg/Ha).<br/>
    -&nbsp;Follow pesticide rotation to avoid pest resistance<br/>-&nbsp;Frequency of spray depends upon the pest intensity<br/>-&nbsp;Use of personal protective equipment (PPE) is recommended while using chemical pesticides<br/>
    -&nbsp;Spray the crop with Spinosad 45 SC at a rate of 0.5 ml/L.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class III (slightly hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Spray with either of the following: Flubendamide 48 SC, Chlorantraniliprole 18.5%, Indoxacarb at 0.1% or HaNPV at 250 LE and a half dose of any of the aforementioned chemicals.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Flubendamide and Indoxacarb WHO Class II (moderately hazardous); Chlorantraniliprole WHO Class U (unlikely to present acute hazards in normal use).<br/>
Scientific Names: <i>Helicoverpa armigera</i> Hubner
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_110882.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_110883.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Deep summer ploughing.<br/>-&nbsp;Early planting can help avoid this pest.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid monocropping. Grow simultaneously 40 day-old African marigold with 25 day-old tomato seedlings, in the 1:16 rows.<br/>-&nbsp;Correctly space your seeds/seedlings. Spacing depends upon variety but in general should be 60 x 45 cm.<br/>-&nbsp;Install bird perches in the field to encourage predatory birds.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural pest enemies (such as spiders, ants, parasitic wasps and robber flies).<br/> -&nbsp;Regular monitoring of the crop to check for caterpillars on the leaves, ideally during morning and evening hours. Threshold is 5-10% of leaves infested.<br/>-&nbsp;Use pheromone and/or light traps to check for the presence of the adult moths. Use 1 trap per acre. Action threshold is 3 or more adult moths in a trap.<br/> -&nbsp;Install pheromomone traps (5 traps per acre) for mass trapping of male moths. Change the lure every 15 days.<br/>-&nbsp;Install light traps to attract and kill adult moths.<br/>-&nbsp;Release the egg parasitoid <i>Trichogramma brassilences</i> or <i>T. pretiosum</i> at a rate of 40000-60000 parasitoids per acre once the action threshold is reached, or at the time of flowering. Repeat at 15-day intervals.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray neem-based insecticides e.g. NSKE (5% or Neem oil at a rate of 3 ml/L of water).<br/>-&nbsp;Foliar spray of the virus HaNPV (250 LE/Ha) with 20 Jaggery in the evening hours.<br/>-&nbsp;Foliar spray of the bacterium <i>Bacillus thuringiensis</i> var. kurstaki (1-1.5 kg/Ha).<br/> -&nbsp;Follow pesticide rotation to avoid pest resistance<br/>-&nbsp;Frequency of spray depends upon the pest intensity<br/>-&nbsp;Use of personal protective equipment (PPE) is recommended while using chemical pesticides<br/> -&nbsp;Spray the crop with Spinosad 45 SC at a rate of 0.5 ml/L.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;WHO Class III (slightly hazardous).<br/>-&nbsp;Spray with either of the following: Flubendamide 48 SC, Chlorantraniliprole 18.5%, Indoxacarb at 0.1% or HaNPV at 250 LE and a half dose of any of the aforementioned chemicals.&nbsp;General Restrictions:&nbsp;Flubendamide and Indoxacarb WHO Class II (moderately hazardous); Chlorantraniliprole WHO Class U (unlikely to present acute hazards in normal use).<br/>

Title: Late blight on tomato
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Late blight is fungi-like mould disease which can affect the leaves, stems and fruits <br/>-&nbsp;Plant resistant varieties if available<br/>-&nbsp;Transplant disease-free seedlings (seedlings without symptoms and not from an infected crop)<br/>-&nbsp;Keep wide plant spacing (80cm X 50cm) to reduce the spread of disease by improving aeration and plant-to-plant contact<br/>-&nbsp;Support the plants with sticks and prune for better aeration. Do not do this when conditions are wet since the disease can be spread by people and tools<br/>-&nbsp;Disinfect hands and tools after working on the infected plants to prevent the spread of the disease to healthy plants. Use household bleach or 5% sodium hypochloride<br/>-&nbsp;Water in the morning so the leaves can dry in the sun<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting during wet and cool periods<br/>-&nbsp;Use dry mulch to reduce water splash and therefore avoid spread of disease<br/>-&nbsp;Apply recommended rates of nitrogen fertilizer as high rates increase likelihood of disease<br/>-&nbsp;Remove and burn debris from tomatoes and other solanaceous crops (potato, peppers and eggplant) after harvest<br/>-&nbsp;Do not plant solaneceous crops (e.g. tomato, Irish potato, pepper, eggplan, nightshade) on land that suffered from the disease in the previous season or near land that currently suffers from the disease<br/>-&nbsp;Practice crop rotation with non-solanaceous crops such as beans, sweet potato, maize, cowpea, soybean, groundnuts, onion, carrots for 2 growing seasons<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor twice at least week for symptoms and on a daily basis during the raining season since wet and humid conditions favour the disease<br/>-&nbsp;Look for irregular greasy greyish patches on the leaves which expand rapdily under damp/humid conditions. These patches are an early symptom of the disease<br/>-&nbsp;Look for brown elongated lesions on the stems and brown-green blotches or spots on the tomato fruits. These are symptoms of the later stages of the disease<br/>-&nbsp;Look for white downy moulds at the margin of affected areas on the lower leaf surface<br/>-&nbsp;When dry weather is expected, some infection symptoms might be tolerated and infected plant parts can be removed. When wet, cool weather (< 24C) is expected, 2-4 plants with symptoms per  acre are enough to consider quick action.<br/>
    -&nbsp;If plant parts show early symptoms, remove the infected plant parts in dry conditions. Carry the diseased materials away in a closed container to prevent spreading the disease. Burn the planting material or bury to at least 1 m deep. Do not compost<br/>-&nbsp;Neem has a slight anti-fungal effect. Spray 50 ml of neem oil with some soap per 10 litres of water within 8 hours of mixing, and every 7-14 days until no more fungal infection appears. Ask your local input suppliers to organise such products if neem trees do not grow in your area.<br/>-&nbsp;If the plants are badly infected/show later symptoms then remove the entire plants in dry conditions. Carry the diseased materials away in a closed container to prevent spreading the disease. Burn the planting material or bury to at least 1 m deep. Do not compost.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Phytophthora infestans</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111008.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111009.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111010.img?width=800&height=640
Content:

Ask your local input suppliers to organise such products if neem trees do not grow in your area.<br/>-&nbsp;If the plants are badly infected/show later symptoms then remove the entire plants in dry conditions. Carry the diseased materials away in a closed container to prevent spreading the disease. Burn the planting material or bury to at least 1 m deep. Do not compost.<br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Aphids on tea
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Actively remove weeds and alternate hosts from the field and surrounding area (including mango, cocoa, coffee and magnolia).<br/>-&nbsp;Maintain sufficient nutrient levels in the soil to ensure promoted plant development to withstand potential aphid infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies such as spiders, parasitic wasps, lacewings and ladybugs).<br/>-&nbsp;Plant resistant varieties if locally available.<br/>-&nbsp;Encourage the presence of natural enemies such as predatory ladybug beetles by planting flowing plants such as marigold as a border crop. Marigold can also act as a trap crop for aphid control.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor crops roughly one week from the seedling stage, observe the underside of leaves and plant stems for aphid presence.<br/>-&nbsp;Adult aphids are roughly 1-2 mm long, with black-brown/red/green bodies.<br/>-&nbsp;Nymphs are roughly 1-1.5 mm long, with lighter black/brown bodies. Both the adults and nymphs are commonly found on the underside of leaves or on stems.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of aphid infestation include distored leaf development, wilting, yellowing and rolling/folding. <br/>-&nbsp;Ants are commonly associated with aphid infestation due to the secretion of honeydew. Black sooty mould may also develop on the honeydew.<br/>-&nbsp;Consider direct control actions when 30% or more of crops show signs of infestations of are found with aphids.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Collect and destroy infested plant parts by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of soap spray solution (1 teaspoon of liquid soap per litre of water) to spray the leaves to remove aphids from the plant.<br/>-&nbsp;Control ant populations using ant bait stations or with tape on the plant stems, this will expose aphids to natural predators.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Toxoptera aurantii</i>, <i>Aphis spiraecola</i>, <i>Aphis gossypii</i>, <i>Myzus persicae</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111035.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111036.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Actively remove weeds and alternate hosts from the field and surrounding area (including mango, cocoa, coffee and magnolia).<br/>-&nbsp;Maintain sufficient nutrient levels in the soil to ensure promoted plant development to withstand potential aphid infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies such as spiders, parasitic wasps, lacewings and ladybugs).<br/>-&nbsp;Plant resistant varieties if locally available.<br/>-&nbsp;Encourage the presence of natural enemies such as predatory ladybug beetles by planting flowing plants such as marigold as a border crop. Title: Fruit flies on banana
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Undertake appropriate phytosanitary procedures. Only use planting materials from approved sources and do not use from areas known to be threatened by fruit fly infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Cover immature banana with polythene or paper bags within one month of flowering to prevent fruit fly accessibility.<br/>-&nbsp;Removal and destruction of plant residue from previous harvests and fallen fruit to kill any larvae present.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect fallen fruit in a black polythene bag. Tie up and leave in the sun to kill any larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Rake and disturb the soil below banana trees to expose pupae to the environment, leading to dessication or predation. <br/>-&nbsp;Harvest fruit at physiological maturity before they ripen to reduce the ability of the flies to lay eggs.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor plantations regularly for fruit fly presence and symptoms, especially during fruiting stage of crops.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are small flies, roughly 5-8 mm long with black and yellow colourations. Larvae are roughly 10 mm long, white maggots which develop into brown pupae.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe fruit for signs of egg-laying, this will be small holes in the surface of the fruit.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect fallen fruit and check inside for the presence of maggots.<br/>-&nbsp;Use 10 pheromone traps within the plantation. If 2-3 adult fruit flies are caught per trap every week then consider direct control actions.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use of heat treatment on fallen fruit to destroy larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of cold treatment (below 15 degrees celsius) on stored fruit for 5 days to kill any fruit flies and/or larvae/pupae living inside fruit. <br/>-&nbsp;Use of neem oil or seed extract (4%) spray on foliage to kill pupating larvae and deter oviposition.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Bactrocera invadens</i>; <i>Ceratitis rosa</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111053.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111054.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Undertake appropriate phytosanitary procedures. Only use planting materials from approved sources and do not use from areas known to be threatened by fruit fly infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Cover immature banana with polythene or paper bags within one month of flowering to prevent fruit fly accessibility.<br/>-&nbsp;Removal and destruction of plant residue from previous harvests and fallen fruit to kill any larvae present.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect fallen fruit in a black polythene bag. Tie up and leave in the sun to kill any larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Rake and disturb the soil below banana trees to expose pupae to the environment, leading to dessication or predation. <br/>-&nbsp;Harvest fruit at physiological maturity before they ripen to reduce the ability of the flies to lay eggs.<br/> -&nbsp;Monitor plantations regularly for fruit fly presence and symptoms, especially during fruiting stage of crops.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are small flies, roughly 5-8 mm long with black and yellow colourations. Larvae are roughly 10 mm long, white maggots which develop into brown pupae.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe fruit for signs of egg-laying, this will be small holes in the surface of the fruit.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect fallen fruit and check inside for the presence of maggots.<br/>-&nbsp;Use 10 pheromone traps within the plantation. If 2-3 adult fruit flies are caught per trap every week then consider direct control actions.<br/> -&nbsp;Use of heat treatment on fallen fruit to destroy larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of cold treatment (below 15 degrees celsius) on stored fruit for 5 days to kill any fruit flies and/or larvae/pupae living inside fruit. <br/>-&nbsp;Use of neem oil or seed extract (4%) spray on foliage to kill pupating larvae and deter oviposition.<br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Fruit fly on cucurbits
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Deep plough the field during winter to expose and damage any overwintering pupae.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake appropriate phytosanitary procedures. Only use planting materials from approved sources and do not use from areas known to be threatened by fruit fly infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Cover immature vegetables with polythene or paper bags within one month of flowering to prevent flruit fly accessibility.<br/>-&nbsp;Removal and destruction of plant residue from previous harvests to kill any larvae present.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect fallen vegetables in a black polythene bag. Tie up and leave in the sun to kill any larvae.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor fields regularly for fruit fly presence and symptoms, especially during fruiting stage of crops.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are small flies, roughly 5-8 mm long with black and yellow colourations. Larvae are roughly 10 mm long, white maggots which develop into brown pupae.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe crops for signs of egg-laying, this will be small holes in the surface of the vegetables.<br/>-&nbsp;Collect fallen crops and check inside for the presence of maggots.<br/>-&nbsp;Use 3-4 pheromone traps (contact local extension officer for advice on what pheromone to use) within the field. If 2-3 adult fruit flies are caught per trap every week then consider direct control actions.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Collect infested and damaged fruit and destroy by burning or burying deep in the ground.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of heat treatment on fallen fruit to destroy larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of cold treatment (below 15 degrees celcius) on stored crops for 5 days to kill any fruit flies and/or larvae/pupae living inside fruit. <br/>-&nbsp;Use of bait pheromone traps to attract fruit flies and reduce populations within field.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Drosophila</i> spp.

Content:

Title: Rice water weevil
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Remove plant material left from previous harvest and weeds in the surrounding area to reduce suitable overwintering habitat.<br/>-&nbsp;Use certified, pest-free seeds.<br/>-&nbsp;Delay permanent flooding of fields by 2-3 weeks to reduce weevil and weed populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Early planting of rice (before mid-April) has been shown to improve crop tolerance of infestations without yield loss.<br/>-&nbsp;Flooding of fields over winter has been shown to reduce weevil populations the following season by up to 50%.<br/>-&nbsp;Clean tools before and after use on your land to reduce human-induced spread of weevils between fields.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies of the pest, such as lacewings, predatory beetles and aphids, and dragonflies.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Observe crops for adult weevils. Note that infestation levels may increase following extended periods of rainfall.<br/>-&nbsp;Adult rice water weevils are small (0.1-4 mm long), grey insects with a prominent 'beak'. Close observations will note dark markings running along the back of adults from the head to the middle of the wing sheaths.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe crops for infestation symptoms:<br/>	  Root pruning, caused by larvae feeding on rice roots, can cause stunted plant growth and yield losses. <br/>	  Adult feeding can be seen from slits on the upper surface of leaves. High levels of feeding on individual plants may result in death.<br/>-&nbsp;Use a floating barrier trap placed in the field immediately after flooding. If 1 weevil is found in a trap every day for the first 7 days, this indicates that damaging populations are likely to develop and control actions should be considered.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use of entomophagous fungi (<i>Beauveria bassiana</i> and <i>Metarhizium anisopliae</i>) to reduce weevil populations.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Lissorhoptrus oryzophilus</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111092.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111093.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Remove plant material left from previous harvest and weeds in the surrounding area to reduce suitable overwintering habitat.<br/>-&nbsp;Use certified, pest-free seeds.<br/>-&nbsp;Delay permanent flooding of fields by 2-3 weeks to reduce weevil and weed populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Early planting of rice (before mid-April) has been shown to improve crop tolerance of infestations without yield loss.<br/>-&nbsp;Flooding of fields over winter has been shown to reduce weevil populations the following season by up to 50%.<br/>-&nbsp;Clean tools before and after use on your land to reduce human-induced spread of weevils between fields.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies of the pest, such as lacewings, predatory beetles and aphids, and dragonflies.<br/> -&nbsp;Observe crops for adult weevils. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Cotton seed bug
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Remove alternate hosts from the field, these include apple, avocado, grapes and pomegranate.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies, including parasitic wasps and lacewings.<br/>-&nbsp;Use okra as a border trap crop to help prevent pests from entering the crop field.<br/>-&nbsp;Harvest cotton as soon as possible so to reduce the period for heavy infestations to occur.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Actively monitor for infestation during growth periods, inspect seeds and internal areas of plants by hand for adults and nymphs.<br/>-&nbsp;Eggs are oval shaped (0.25-0.1 mm), pale yellow/pink and found laid close to seeds in clutches of 2-4 or individually.<br/>-&nbsp;Nymphs have pink/orange abdomens and brown heads. Brown colouration darkens during development (14-20 day period). Primarily found on seeds as this is their food source.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are 3.8-4.3 mm long, with brown/black colourations across the body. Males have rounded abdomens and females have elongated, trunk-shaped abdomens. Notably the adult has pale green/yellow striped on the legs and antennae.<br/>-&nbsp;As the pest feeds on seeds, seed germination may be reduced by up to 88% in high infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Common symptoms of infestation are pink lint as insects are crushed in cotton plants, large numbers of the insect on neighbouring buildings, foul odour emitting from insects.<br/>
    -&nbsp;In the early morning, walk through the field and brush off any adults found on the plants, crush any fallen adults to kill.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove heavily infested plant parts and destroy by burning away from the field.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of biocontrol agents such as the entomopathogenic fungi <i>Beauverua bassiana</i> and <i>Metarhizium anisopliae</i>. Contact your local extension officer for further advice on control options available in your area.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Oxycarenus hyalinipennis</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111151.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111152.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Remove alternate hosts from the field, these include apple, avocado, grapes and pomegranate.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies, including parasitic wasps and lacewings.<br/>-&nbsp;Use okra as a border trap crop to help prevent pests from entering the crop field.<br/>-&nbsp;Harvest cotton as soon as possible so to reduce the period for heavy infestations to occur.<br/> -&nbsp;Actively monitor for infestation during growth periods, inspect seeds and internal areas of plants by hand for adults and nymphs.<br/>-&nbsp;Eggs are oval shaped (0.25-0.1 mm), pale yellow/pink and found laid close to seeds in clutches of 2-4 or individually.<br/>-&nbsp;Nymphs have pink/orange abdomens and brown heads. Title: Cutworms on tea
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Avoid planting crops in or near fields with a known history of cutworm infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Prior to planting, plough the field to expose any cutworm larvae to the sun and natural enemies.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove weeds from the plantation and neighbouring areas to reduce host plant availability.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies such as parasitic wasps, ants, spiders and lacewings.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant sunflowers as a border around tea plantations as a trap crop.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor for cutworm activity during the early morning and late evening as this is when larvae are most active above ground.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of cutworm infestation include feeding activity on leaves, stalks and stems by larvae which can result if heavy defoliation. Larvae can also feed on roots and tubers, leaving small holes. <br/>-&nbsp;Damage is more severe under dry conditions, deeper below the soil surface.<br/>-&nbsp;Eggs are found stuck to plant material and on soil, pale red/pink in colour and roughly 0.5 mm in diameter.<br/>-&nbsp;Larvae are 2-4 cm long, with dark brown/grey heads and cream/brown bodies. <br/>-&nbsp;Pupa are 20-30 mm long, yellow/brown which darkens during development.<br/>-&nbsp;Adult moths have a wingspan of 4-45 mm with grey/brown wings. Females have grey hind wings and males have silver/white hind wings.<br/>-&nbsp;Consider direct control actions as soon as larval activity is identified.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Handpick larvae during the early morning and late evening during low infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Use biological control measures such as the release of <i>Trichogramma</i> spp. Release weekly for 3 weeks as soon as adult moths are identified.<br/>-&nbsp;Apply a paste solution of molasses, sawdust and eggshells around the base of plant stems to kill larvae before they can climb up and eat away at the crops.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Agrotis segetum</i>, <i>Agrotis ipsilon</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111153.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111154.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111155.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Avoid planting crops in or near fields with a known history of cutworm infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Prior to planting, plough the field to expose any cutworm larvae to the sun and natural enemies.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove weeds from the plantation and neighbouring areas to reduce host plant availability.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies such as parasitic wasps, ants, spiders and lacewings.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant sunflowers as a border around tea plantations as a trap crop.<br/> -&nbsp;Monitor for cutworm activity during the early morning and late evening as this is when larvae are most active above ground.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of cutworm infestation include feeding activity on leaves, stalks and stems by larvae which can result if heavy defoliation. Title: Red spider mite on coffee
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Use resistant crop varieties, contact local extension officer for advice on locally available varieties.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant nurseries away from areas of known infestation.<br/>-&nbsp;Apply mulch to improve health of crops and reduce impact from potential infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant perennial hedges (e.g. pigeon pea) to attract predatory mites to kill <i>O. coffeae</i>.<br/>-&nbsp;Actively remove weeds form plantations to reduce suitable habitat for mite infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertale suitable phytosanitary practices, wash clothing and tools used in infested areas before entering new ones.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural predators such as predatory mites, spiders, lacewings, and ladybird beetles.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Actively monitor crops for mite activity, note that it may be hard to distinguish between mite infestation symptoms and nutrient deficiency. If so, observe 10 random leaves per 20 plants for small red mites.<br/>-&nbsp;Mite colonies are commonly found on the supper surface of old leaves, but may move to either surface of young leaves under drought conditions and heavy infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Mites are around 360m long, with dark red, oval bodies. Males may appear to have a slightly elongated dorsal end. <br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of infestation include yellow spots on leaves, turning brown and necrotic under heavy infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Defoliation may occur in heavily infested plants.<br/>-&nbsp;When mites are identified or symptoms first appear, consider undertaking direct control actions.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Remove infested plant parts and destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of a soap spray (3 tablespoons of liquid soap with 4 litres of water). Apply during the early morning or late afternoon.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of neem seed kernel extract spray (grind 500 g of neem seed kernels and mix with 10 litres of water). Spray during dry periods as rainfall will wash off the solution.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of flour solution spray (2 cups of flour with 5-10 litres of water). Apply to the underside of leaves, when the solution dries during the day any mites present will be encrusted in flour and die. <br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Oligonychus coffeae</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111158.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111159.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111160.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Use resistant crop varieties, contact local extension officer for advice on locally available varieties.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant nurseries away from areas of known infestation.<br/>-&nbsp;Apply mulch to improve health of crops and reduce impact from potential infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant perennial hedges (e.g. pigeon pea) to attract predatory mites to kill <i>O. coffeae</i>.<br/>-&nbsp;Actively remove weeds form plantations to reduce suitable habitat for mite infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertale suitable phytosanitary practices, wash clothing and tools used in infested areas before entering new ones.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural predators such as predatory mites, spiders, lacewings, and ladybird beetles.<br/> -&nbsp;Actively monitor crops for mite activity, note that it may be hard to distinguish between mite infestation symptoms and nutrient deficiency. Title: Black cutworm on cotton
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Avoid planting cotton in fields previously used to grow alternate host crops, such as chickpea, groundnut and onion.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting cotton in areas which were previously meadow or pasture land.<br/>-&nbsp;Use certified, disinfected seeds to avoid introduction of the pest into your field.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove weeds to reduce egg presence during the growing season.<br/>-&nbsp;Flood the field for 6 hours during the seeding stage (after germination) to drown any caterpillars, wait 2-4 days before soil preparation when the soil has dried. Subsequent irrigation (3-6 hours) will vary based on soil type and climate.<br/>-&nbsp;Kill and burn post-harvest plant material and wait at least 2 months before further use to kill any pupae or other insect stages.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid intercropping/growing near to host crops such as maize.<br/>-&nbsp;Plough in the Autumn and use shallow tillage to reduce late autumn/early spring plant growth.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies such as spiders, minute pirate bugs, damselflies and lacewing larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Consult and follow local dates for sowing, killing/burning, phytosanitary control and weed control.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Cutworms are small (1-2 cm long), grey caterpillars which primarily feed on seedlings and young plants during the night. In the day they are found burrowed in the soil or plant stems.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe seedlings for shotholes caused by young larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Later stage instars become active only at night. Observe the base of plants and surrounding soil for cutworm presence. <br/>-&nbsp;During plant growth stages, note that cutworm larvae can burrow into stems, so observe for boreholes.<br/>-&nbsp;Cutworm caterpillars are light/dark grey with no other markings and can grow up to 1-2 cm long.<br/>-&nbsp;Adult moths have a black dagger shape on the outer margins of the wings. <br/>-&nbsp;If 5-10 caterpillars are identified per 10 plants then consider undertaking direct control actions.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Actively remove weeds in fields to reduce available space for egg laying. Destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;Irrigate land for short periods (3-6 hours) to kill pupae and reduce cutworm larvae feeding activity. <br/>-&nbsp;Search the undergrowth of fields and soil around the base of seedlings. Actively remove and destroy larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of <i>Bacillus thuringiensis</i> to reduce populations of larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Use yellow blacklight traps (during summer) and pheromone traps to control adult populations. <br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Agrotis ipsilon</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111169.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111170.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111171.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Avoid planting cotton in fields previously used to grow alternate host crops, such as chickpea, groundnut and onion.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting cotton in areas which were previously meadow or pasture land.<br/>-&nbsp;Use certified, disinfected seeds to avoid introduction of the pest into your field.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove weeds to reduce egg presence during the growing season.<br/>-&nbsp;Flood the field for 6 hours during the seeding stage (after germination) to drown any caterpillars, wait 2-4 days before soil preparation when the soil has dried. In the day they are found burrowed in the soil or plant stems.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe seedlings for shotholes caused by young larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Later stage instars become active only at night. Title: Chironomus on rice
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Reduce the slope of paddocks to create uniform, shallow water to reduce potential infestations of bloodworm larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake appropriate tillage of land prior to planting to expose any larvae in the soil to natural enemies.<br/>-&nbsp;Soil management practices such as crushing and removing plant material by a tractor with paddle wheels.<br/>-&nbsp;Flooding and sowing of smaller paddock areas are suggested to reduce bloodworm infestation and crop damage.<br/>-&nbsp;Use pre-germinated seeds to reduce the risk period of seed attacks by larvae as the pest doesn't damage secondary root systems.<br/>-&nbsp;Use independent irrigation in nurseries to prevent the spread of pest.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Bloodworm larvae have distinct dark heads and can grow up to 18 mm long. Larvae are commonly red but can also be variations of brown and black.<br/>-&nbsp;Aquatic earthworms are commonly misidentified as bloodworms. However, they are much larger, growing up to 75 mm long and lack a distinct head capsule.<br/>-&nbsp;Larvae can burrow into seeds and consume the endosperm, feed on shoots and seedlings, and attach the root system (causing stunted growth).<br/>-&nbsp;High infestation levels of larvae can destabilize soil surfaces, resulting in poor plant anchorage. <br/>-&nbsp;Adults are pale green/brown midges (up to 15 mm long) with long antennae.<br/>-&nbsp;To monitor larval populations, take a soil sample using a core sampler or a kitchen ladle. Take 3 core samples, if the number of larvae in 2 of the 3 samples are greater than 10 (serious infestation) then consider direct control actions immediately.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Periodic applications of irrigation and dry conditions throughout the vegetative stage of crop after transplanting.<br/>-&nbsp;Contact your local agro-dealer or agriculture agency for approved treatments of the pest in your area.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake fangueo post-harvest to reduce the likelihood of larvae surviving into the following season.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Chironomus</i> spp.
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111172.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111173.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Reduce the slope of paddocks to create uniform, shallow water to reduce potential infestations of bloodworm larvae.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake appropriate tillage of land prior to planting to expose any larvae in the soil to natural enemies.<br/>-&nbsp;Soil management practices such as crushing and removing plant material by a tractor with paddle wheels.<br/>-&nbsp;Flooding and sowing of smaller paddock areas are suggested to reduce bloodworm infestation and crop damage.<br/>-&nbsp;Use pre-germinated seeds to reduce the risk period of seed attacks by larvae as the pest doesn't damage secondary root systems.<br/>-&nbsp;Use independent irrigation in nurseries to prevent the spread of pest.<br/> -&nbsp;Bloodworm larvae have distinct dark heads and can grow up to 18 mm long. Larvae are commonly red but can also be variations of brown and black.<br/>-&nbsp;Aquatic earthworms are commonly misidentified as bloodworms. However, they are much larger, growing up to 75 mm long and lack a distinct head capsule.<br/>-&nbsp;Larvae can burrow into seeds and consume the endosperm, feed on shoots and seedlings, and attach the root system (causing stunted growth).<br/>-&nbsp;High infestation levels of larvae can destabilize soil surfaces, resulting in poor plant anchorage. <br/>-&nbsp;Adults are pale green/brown midges (up to 15 mm long) with long antennae.<br/>-&nbsp;To monitor larval populations, take a soil sample using a core sampler or a kitchen ladle. Take 3 core samples, if the number of larvae in 2 of the 3 samples are greater than 10 (serious infestation) then consider direct control actions immediately.<br/> -&nbsp;Periodic applications of irrigation and dry conditions throughout the vegetative stage of crop after transplanting.<br/>-&nbsp;Contact your local agro-dealer or agriculture agency for approved treatments of the pest in your area.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake fangueo post-harvest to reduce the likelihood of larvae surviving into the following season.<br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Rice thrips
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Remove plant residue from previous harvest to reduce suitable habitat for pest overwintering.<br/>-&nbsp;Use certified, resistant seed varieties.<br/>-&nbsp;Plough soil 2-3 times before planting to control weed populations which could host thrip populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Flood rice field to submerge rice seedlings and young plants for 2 days to control thrip populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Optimum seed rate (74 kg/Ha) should be used to avoid dense vegetation. Seed rates vary according to seedbed preparation method. <br/>-&nbsp;Avoid water stress conditions, maintain the optimum level of water in the nursery and field.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies (e.g. dragonflies, spiders, waterbugs, predatory thrips, and ladybugs).<br/>
    -&nbsp;Regularly monitor fields 3 times per week for the first 30 days after transplanting.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe crops for damage on leaves and stems and leaf rolling, which are signs of thrip activity.<br/>-&nbsp;Typical symptoms also include stunted plant growth, wilted leaves with fine yellow lines or/and silver streaks and seedling death (in severe infestations).<br/>-&nbsp;Young plants (1-2 weeks after transplanting) are most affected by thrip infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Using wet hands, brush hands across the top of plants. Any thrips present will stick to your hand.<br/>-&nbsp;If 10-20 thrips are observed on your hand, then consider direct control actions.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Under high levels of thrip infestation, flood the field for 2 days (especially when rice plants are young as they are most at risk from damage at this stage).<br/>-&nbsp;Flood the nursery and drain after 15 minutes.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Stenchaetothrips biformis</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111176.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111177.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Remove plant residue from previous harvest to reduce suitable habitat for pest overwintering.<br/>-&nbsp;Use certified, resistant seed varieties.<br/>-&nbsp;Plough soil 2-3 times before planting to control weed populations which could host thrip populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Flood rice field to submerge rice seedlings and young plants for 2 days to control thrip populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Optimum seed rate (74 kg/Ha) should be used to avoid dense vegetation. Seed rates vary according to seedbed preparation method. <br/>-&nbsp;Avoid water stress conditions, maintain the optimum level of water in the nursery and field.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies (e.g. dragonflies, spiders, waterbugs, predatory thrips, and ladybugs).<br/> -&nbsp;Regularly monitor fields 3 times per week for the first 30 days after transplanting.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe crops for damage on leaves and stems and leaf rolling, which are signs of thrip activity.<br/>-&nbsp;Typical symptoms also include stunted plant growth, wilted leaves with fine yellow lines or/and silver streaks and seedling death (in severe infestations).<br/>-&nbsp;Young plants (1-2 weeks after transplanting) are most affected by thrip infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Using wet hands, brush hands across the top of plants. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Sheath blight on rice
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Increase plant spacing (25x25 cm) to increase aeration and reduce humidity which is favourable for fungal spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Practise appropriate use of fertiliser to promote plant growth, and reduce impacts of fungal infections. Overuse of nitrogenous fertilizers may damage crops and make them more susceptible to pest damage.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove weeds from fields and neighbouring areas to reduce availability of hosts for fungal spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Appropriate land management (i.e. deep ploughing and levelling) to promote fungus-free soil.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid high seeding rates.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitoring should start from panicle differentiation until heading. Scout crops 3 times a week for signs of fungi, especially during warm wet periods.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of sheath rice blight include round lesions (1-3 cm long) on leaf sheaths or dark green spots located at the bottom of rice plants. The centers of the spots turn yellow/grey/white before turning brown.<br/>-&nbsp;If fungal infection is identified throughout the field then consider taking direct control measures.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Remove infected plant material and destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;In heavily infected areas, drain the field and allow to dry. Increase circulation between plants and allow good sun coverage to suppress further fungal development and spread.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Rhizoctonia solani</i> 
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111178.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111179.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Increase plant spacing (25x25 cm) to increase aeration and reduce humidity which is favourable for fungal spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Practise appropriate use of fertiliser to promote plant growth, and reduce impacts of fungal infections. Overuse of nitrogenous fertilizers may damage crops and make them more susceptible to pest damage.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove weeds from fields and neighbouring areas to reduce availability of hosts for fungal spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Appropriate land management (i.e. deep ploughing and levelling) to promote fungus-free soil.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid high seeding rates.<br/> -&nbsp;Monitoring should start from panicle differentiation until heading. The centers of the spots turn yellow/grey/white before turning brown.<br/>-&nbsp;If fungal infection is identified throughout the field then consider taking direct control measures.<br/> -&nbsp;Remove infected plant material and destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;In heavily infected areas, drain the field and allow to dry. Title: Aphids on beans
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Plant crop in high densities, as sparce planting increases aphid infestation (80 kg/Ha seed rate).<br/>-&nbsp;Prior to planting, remove all weeds to reduce aphid hosts in field area.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove nearby host plants and weeds to reduce spread of aphid infestations from other areas into fields.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant winter crops early (May-June).<br/>-&nbsp;Intercropping with maize to reduce aphid infestation.<br/>-&nbsp;Use appropriate irrigation in fields, but avoid excessive nitrogen levels in the soil.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor crops once or twice a week during dry periods and before plant flowering.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe for colonies of small green/black insects, notably around the stems, growing points and the underside of leaves. <br/>-&nbsp;Sooty mould is a characteristic feature of aphid infestation, it grows on sticky honeydew produced by aphids.<br/>-&nbsp;Leaf distortion and curling is a sign of aphid feeding.<br/>-&nbsp;Ants are commonly associated with aphid infestation as they are attracted to the honeydew. <br/>-&nbsp;Use yellow sticky and water pan traps to monitor for aphids.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake direct control methods 15-20% plants are found to be infested.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Prune leaves showing infestation symptoms.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant flowering plants as a border to attract natural enemies (e.g. wasps and syriphids).<br/>-&nbsp;Spray crops with strong jets of water to remove aphids from plants.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray plants with insecticidal soap mixed with water (ratio of 1:9) once a week. <br/>-&nbsp;Spray with neem oil products once a week for 4 weeks (4.5 ml/10 m2). Contact your local agro-input supplier to organise access of such products.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Aphis fabae</i>, <i>A. craccivora</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111185.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111186.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111187.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Plant crop in high densities, as sparce planting increases aphid infestation (80 kg/Ha seed rate).<br/>-&nbsp;Prior to planting, remove all weeds to reduce aphid hosts in field area.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove nearby host plants and weeds to reduce spread of aphid infestations from other areas into fields.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant winter crops early (May-June).<br/>-&nbsp;Intercropping with maize to reduce aphid infestation.<br/>-&nbsp;Use appropriate irrigation in fields, but avoid excessive nitrogen levels in the soil.<br/> -&nbsp;Monitor crops once or twice a week during dry periods and before plant flowering.<br/>-&nbsp;Observe for colonies of small green/black insects, notably around the stems, growing points and the underside of leaves. <br/>-&nbsp;Sooty mould is a characteristic feature of aphid infestation, it grows on sticky honeydew produced by aphids.<br/>-&nbsp;Leaf distortion and curling is a sign of aphid feeding.<br/>-&nbsp;Ants are commonly associated with aphid infestation as they are attracted to the honeydew. <br/>-&nbsp;Use yellow sticky and water pan traps to monitor for aphids.<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake direct control methods 15-20% plants are found to be infested.<br/> -&nbsp;Prune leaves showing infestation symptoms.<br/>-&nbsp;Plant flowering plants as a border to attract natural enemies (e.g. wasps and syriphids).<br/>-&nbsp;Spray crops with strong jets of water to remove aphids from plants.<br/>-&nbsp;Spray plants with insecticidal soap mixed with water (ratio of 1:9) once a week. <br/>-&nbsp;Spray with neem oil products once a week for 4 weeks (4.5 ml/10 m2). Title: Aphids on watermelon
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Remove weeds and alternate hosts (such as citrus and other solonaceous crops) from the field and surrounding area.<br/>-&nbsp;Intercropping with onion or garlic to reduce aphid populations on your crop.<br/>-&nbsp;Separate early and late planting areas to minise the risk of aphids spreading from one field to another.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural enemies such as parasitic wasps, spiders and ladybugs.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of resistant varieties to aphid infestations if locally available.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of row covers to prevent early infestation and potential virus transmission, covers can be left on crops until flowering stage to protect from pest activity.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor for aphids weekly  or a week after crops are planted, observe the underside of leaves of runners for insect presence.<br/>-&nbsp;Adult aphids are light/dark green, pear-shaped, grow up to 1-1.5 mm long and are commonly found on the underside of leaves and on stems.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of aphid infestation include yellowing of leaves, stunted growth and leaf curling.<br/>-&nbsp;Sooty black mould may also be observed during aphid infestations due to the secretion of honeydew.<br/>-&nbsp;If 20% or more of runners are infested then consider direct control actions.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Use of parasitic wasps as biocontrol (such as <i>Lysiphlebus testaceipes</i>).<br/>-&nbsp;Prune heavily infested plant parts and destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of soap spray (1 tablespoon of liquid soap with 1 litre of water) on all leaf surfaces to kill aphids.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of garlic spray (one garlic bulb, 1 litre of water, 1 tablespoon of liquid soap and 1 teaspoon of cayenne pepper powder). This solution will last for a week in the refrigerator.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of stinging nettle spray (fill a quarter of a container with nettle and cover in water, leave to ferment for 3-4 weeks. Mix 1 part of the fermented solution with 7 parts water) to repel aphids.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Aphis</i> spp.
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111188.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111189.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111190.img?width=800&height=640
Content:

Title: Bean seed fly
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Bean seed fly is a pest that feed on the swollen, ungerminated seed of vegetable crops at larval stage, called maggot.<br/>-&nbsp;The use of winter cover crops (e.g. grasses) may increase fly populations, if this is needed then plant bean crop 3.5 weeks after the cover crop has been incorportated into the soil.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting during wet periods to reduce infestation of maggots. Plant crops when the weather and soil promotes rapid germination to reduce impact of maggots.<br/>-&nbsp;Remove flowering weeds from the area to reduce nearby food sources for flies.<br/>
    -&nbsp;When monitoring fields, look out for wilting plants and gaps in vegetation where seedlings have failed to emerge. To identify maggots, search in the soil around these areas. <br/>-&nbsp;Maggots can also be found tunneling up the stem of newly emerged seedlings.<br/>-&nbsp;Eggs: 1-2 mm long, white eggs.<br/>-&nbsp;Larvae: Up to 10 mm long, white maggots. Pupae are brown and are up to 7 mm long.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults: Small, grey bodied flies, up to 5.2 mm long.<br/>-&nbsp;The symptoms can be confused with wireworm feeding injury and dieback<br/>
    -&nbsp;The only method for controlling <i>D. platura</i> is targeting the maggot stage. Seed treatments are the cheapest and most effective control for maggots.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Delia platura</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111191.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111192.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111193.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Bean seed fly is a pest that feed on the swollen, ungerminated seed of vegetable crops at larval stage, called maggot.<br/>-&nbsp;The use of winter cover crops (e.g. grasses) may increase fly populations, if this is needed then plant bean crop 3.5 weeks after the cover crop has been incorportated into the soil.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid planting during wet periods to reduce infestation of maggots. Title: Citrus blackfly
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Remove weeds and alternate hosts of blackfly (e.g. guava and pomegranate) from neighbouring areas to reduce the presence of blackfly populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Improve spacing and air circulation within citrus orchards by pruning infested plant areas to reduce suitable habitat for blackflies and population spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of wide spectrum pesticides so to conserve the natural enemies of blackfly such as parasitoids, ladybird and lacewing.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Monitor citrus trees for symptoms of blackfly infestation, also observe trees for distored leaves and ant presence.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of blackfly infestation include sticky honeydew accumlating on leaves and stems and the appearance of sooty black mould. Ants are commonly associated with the presence of blackflies due to the honeydew deposits.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are 1-1.7 mm long, black/grey insects. Young adults are initially orange bodied which darkens over time. The wings have pale markings in a band, with the legs having pale-yellow markings.<br/>-&nbsp;Larvae and pupal stages can be observed as sedintary, small black scales attached to the underside of leaves.<br/>-&nbsp;Eggs are found on the underside of leaves, they are golden brown and darken prior to hatching.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Control ant populations to reduce their ability to protect blackflies from natural enemies. <br/>-&nbsp;Use of parasitic wasps (e.g. <i>Encarsia perplexa</i> and <i>Amitus hesperidum</i> to reduce blackfly populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of fungi <i>Ascgersoni aleurodis</i> or <i>Aegerita webberi</i> to control larvae and pupae.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of neem seed extract spray (4%) to reduce blackfly populations and not harm natural enemies. <br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Aleurocanthus woglumi</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111194.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111195.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111196.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Remove weeds and alternate hosts of blackfly (e.g. guava and pomegranate) from neighbouring areas to reduce the presence of blackfly populations.<br/>-&nbsp;Improve spacing and air circulation within citrus orchards by pruning infested plant areas to reduce suitable habitat for blackflies and population spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of wide spectrum pesticides so to conserve the natural enemies of blackfly such as parasitoids, ladybird and lacewing.<br/> -&nbsp;Monitor citrus trees for symptoms of blackfly infestation, also observe trees for distored leaves and ant presence.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of blackfly infestation include sticky honeydew accumlating on leaves and stems and the appearance of sooty black mould. Title: Cotton aphid
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Rotate planting of cotton with onion or maize. Be sure not to plant alternate hosts such as cucumber or tomato.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of resistant varieties. Contact local extension officer of agro-dealer for locally available varieties.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural pest species such as ladybird beetles, lacewings and parasitoids.<br/>-&nbsp;Top the plants following boll opening to remove the upper leaves where aphids primarily feed. This reduces infestation on lower areas of the plant.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Actively monitor crops for symptoms of infestation and aphid presence by eye and using a hand lens.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of aphid infestation include yellowing and curling of leaves. Stunted growth and distortion of plant stems. <br/>-&nbsp;A characteristic feature of infestation is the presence of black sooty mould growing on the honeydew secretion from aphids.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are 2 mm long, with black heads and yellow/green bodied. <br/>-&nbsp;Nymphs are pale yellow/green, roughly 1-1.5 mm long.<br/>-&nbsp;Consider direct control actions if more than 5 aphids are found per plant when 20 are surveyed.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Using a strong jet of water, wash aphids off the underside of leaves during early and low infestations roughly once a week.<br/>-&nbsp;Prune heavily infested plant parts and destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;Control ant populations, as ants are associated with aphids due to their honeydew secretions and protect aphids from natural enemies. Use a band of sticky tape around the base of plants to trap ants.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of neem extract (50 g neem seed cake per litre of water and 2 tablespoons of liquid soap). Spray no more than twice per season.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of liquid soap solution (10 ml liquid soap, 85 g chopped/ground garlic, 1 litre of water and 50 ml of vegetable oil). Add 2 litres of water to the solution before use then spray.<br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Aphis gossypii</i>
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111197.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111198.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Rotate planting of cotton with onion or maize. Be sure not to plant alternate hosts such as cucumber or tomato.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of resistant varieties. Contact local extension officer of agro-dealer for locally available varieties.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of broad spectrum insecticides to conserve natural pest species such as ladybird beetles, lacewings and parasitoids.<br/>-&nbsp;Top the plants following boll opening to remove the upper leaves where aphids primarily feed. This reduces infestation on lower areas of the plant.<br/> -&nbsp;Actively monitor crops for symptoms of infestation and aphid presence by eye and using a hand lens.<br/>-&nbsp;Symptoms of aphid infestation include yellowing and curling of leaves. Stunted growth and distortion of plant stems. <br/>-&nbsp;A characteristic feature of infestation is the presence of black sooty mould growing on the honeydew secretion from aphids.<br/>-&nbsp;Adults are 2 mm long, with black heads and yellow/green bodied. <br/>-&nbsp;Nymphs are pale yellow/green, roughly 1-1.5 mm long.<br/>-&nbsp;Consider direct control actions if more than 5 aphids are found per plant when 20 are surveyed.<br/> -&nbsp;Using a strong jet of water, wash aphids off the underside of leaves during early and low infestations roughly once a week.<br/>-&nbsp;Prune heavily infested plant parts and destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;Control ant populations, as ants are associated with aphids due to their honeydew secretions and protect aphids from natural enemies. Use a band of sticky tape around the base of plants to trap ants.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of neem extract (50 g neem seed cake per litre of water and 2 tablespoons of liquid soap). Spray no more than twice per season.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of liquid soap solution (10 ml liquid soap, 85 g chopped/ground garlic, 1 litre of water and 50 ml of vegetable oil). Add 2 litres of water to the solution before use then spray.<br/> Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.

Title: Root-knot nematodes in coffee
Authors:   Plantwise
Editors: 
Sections:
    -&nbsp;Use of healthy, certified seeds to prevent the use of infested seeds in fields.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of resistant varieties such as Nemaya or hybrids (contact local extension office for advice on locally available options).<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake suitable phytosanitary actions, clean machines and tool after use in a field.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of surface irrigation from infested fields to healthy ones.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of border trap plants such as marigold to attract nematodes away from coffee crop.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of border trap plants such as marigold to attract nematodes away from coffee crop.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Actively monitor plantation and nurseries for symptoms of nematode infestation.<br/>-&nbsp;Characteristic symptoms of nematode infestations are root knots on the roots, therefore focus monitoring primarily on seedlings and young plants. <br/>-&nbsp;In mature plants, symptoms include stunted growth, wilting and yellowing of leaves. Severe infestation may cause plants to turn brown and die.<br/>-&nbsp;Consider direct control actions as soon as symptoms are observed.<br/>
    -&nbsp;Uproot and destroy heavily infected plants and destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;Add compost to the soil to reduce the impact of nematode infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Actively remove weeds and infested crop material to reduce the likelihood of infestation spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Deep plough soil to expose any nematodes to the environment (e.g. the sun) to kill pests.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of neem cake in the soil to reduce the reproductive ability of nematodes.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of basil leaf extract spray (grind 50 g of basil leaves, soak overnight in 2-3 litres of water. Strain solution and add 10 ml of liquid soap). <br/>
    Pesticides may be available to control this pest. Please check with the Ministry of Agriculture in your country to find out which pesticides are registered in your country and the local restrictions for their use.
Scientific Names: <i>Meloidogyne</i> spp.
Images: ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111203.img?width=800&height=640, ../../../../content/img/resize/PMDG_111204.img?width=800&height=640
Content:
-&nbsp;Use of healthy, certified seeds to prevent the use of infested seeds in fields.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of resistant varieties such as Nemaya or hybrids (contact local extension office for advice on locally available options).<br/>-&nbsp;Undertake suitable phytosanitary actions, clean machines and tool after use in a field.<br/>-&nbsp;Avoid the use of surface irrigation from infested fields to healthy ones.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of border trap plants such as marigold to attract nematodes away from coffee crop.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of border trap plants such as marigold to attract nematodes away from coffee crop.<br/> -&nbsp;Actively monitor plantation and nurseries for symptoms of nematode infestation.<br/>-&nbsp;Characteristic symptoms of nematode infestations are root knots on the roots, therefore focus monitoring primarily on seedlings and young plants. <br/>-&nbsp;In mature plants, symptoms include stunted growth, wilting and yellowing of leaves. Severe infestation may cause plants to turn brown and die.<br/>-&nbsp;Consider direct control actions as soon as symptoms are observed.<br/> -&nbsp;Uproot and destroy heavily infected plants and destroy by burning.<br/>-&nbsp;Add compost to the soil to reduce the impact of nematode infestations.<br/>-&nbsp;Actively remove weeds and infested crop material to reduce the likelihood of infestation spread.<br/>-&nbsp;Deep plough soil to expose any nematodes to the environment (e.g. the sun) to kill pests.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of neem cake in the soil to reduce the reproductive ability of nematodes.<br/>-&nbsp;Use of basil leaf extract spray (grind 50 g of basil leaves, soak overnight in 2-3 litres of water.
